Preface
The work for this master’s thesis was carried out at the International Centre for
Indoor Environment and Energy (ICIEE), Department of Civil Engineering at the
Technical University of Denmark (DTU) in the period from 25th of January to 25th
of June 2016 under the supervision of professor Ph.D. Bjarne W. Olesen, Ph.D. stu-
dent Ongun Berk Kazanci and Ph.D. student Eleftherios Bourdakis. This master’s
thesis accounts for a total of 60 ECTS points, corresponding to a full time semester
for two students.
In this thesis, the APA citation style is used. In-line references are made with
the author-year style, where n.d. indicates that no publication date is available.
A USB ﬂash drive is enclosed. On the ﬂash drive can be found a pdf-version of
this report, TRNSYS simulation models and output ﬁles, Visual Basic and Matlab
scripts and raw data from the experiments.
We wish to express our gratitude towards the people who have helped us and
who made this project possible. First and foremost, we would like to thank our
supervisors for having given us the opportunity to pursue this exciting subject and
for their guidance along the way.
We would also like to thank professor Arno Dentel of Technische Hochschule,
Nürnberg, for generously helping us out, during the TRNSYS simulations. Also, the
whole TRNSYS community deserves our acknowledgement for their patience and
diligence in answering our many questions.
Finally, we wish to express our gratitude to stud.PB. Dorottya Kalmár and M.Sc.
Christian Orthmann for their invaluable help in proofreading this thesis.
Thank you Nathalie and Dorottya, for your support and words of encouragement.
Technical University of Denmark
Kongens Lyngby
25th of June, 2016

==PAGE:4==
ii
Abstract
The aim of this thesis was to create a simpliﬁed method for design of phase
change material (PCM) ceilings in oﬃce buildings.
A parametric study was done for two 2-person oﬃces with PCM ceilings
in a building, with nocturnal discharging by ventilation. The purpose was to
investigate the eﬀect of diﬀerent PCM types and load cases on energy use for
heating, cooling and ventilation. The parametric study was performed using
the simulation software TRNSYS. A total of 2240 simulations with PCM were
computed for the climate of Madrid, Dori, Copenhagen, Buenos Aires and Santa
Maria. The window size, internal heat gains, PCM type, PCM board thickness,
PCM surface area and PCM thermal conductivity were varied. The PCM cases
were compared to 20 reference cases to calculate energy savings.
The simulation models were validated against a benchmark test and several
experiments performed in a climate chamber, which was comparable to the
oﬃce. The study revealed that PCM ceilings have a potential for energy savings
of up to 25.7%, but also that there is a risk of increasing energy use, if the chosen
PCM does not suit the climate or load case. Even though the energy savings
potential was high, the high cost of PCM products resulted in payback times
of more than 20 years, for the majority of the climates.
The thesis concludes with a short recommendation on how to choose PCM
type and key properties, and a statistical model for estimating energy savings
of PCM ceilings in oﬃce buildings.

Chapter 1
Introduction
1
==PAGE:14==
2
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
1.1
Background
There is a global concern for environment changes due to green house gas (GHG)
emissions, and many countries strive to reduce energy use. According to a newly
released report by European Commission (2015), the building sector accounts for
41% of the total energy use in the European Union (EU), split between residential
buildings (27%) and commercial buildings (14%). Similar statistics for the building
sector can be found for the U.S (41%) (U.S. Department of Energy, 2012) and China
(28%) (Yu et al., 2014). Space heating and cooling is responsible for a large part of
the building energy use.
In order to aid the overall reduction of energy use, EU agreed on the "Climate
and Energy Package" with a commitment to reduce GHG-emissions by 20% and
increase the energy eﬃciency and renewable energy sources by 20% by 2020. One of
the identiﬁed ways to reach the goal is to improve the energy eﬃciency in buildings
(European Union, 2008).
The building thermal mass plays an important role in the overall energy use for
space heating and cooling in buildings, by absorbing excess heat during the day, and
releasing it again during night time. Traditionally, the thermal mass in buildings
has been in the form of heavy concrete and masonry building elements, but new
sources of thermal mass are needed in modern lightweight buildings.
A promising technology for lightweight thermal mass enhancement of buildings
is phase change materials (PCM) incorporated into building elements like gypsum
drywall and ceiling boards. PCM-enhanced building materials have been an area of
research for the past 40 years, but the technology has not yet found its way to the
buildings on a broader scale. Many diﬀerent building products have emerged on the
market in the past decade, but the engineers and architects do not have the required
tools to take advantage of this technology (Haghighat et al., 2013).
Soares et al. (2013) conducted a review and found that it is a challenge to evalu-
ate the energy performance of a whole building utilising PCM, because of the many
design parameters. They recognize that PCM-enhanced products can reduce en-
ergy use, "but further research work should be done to ﬁnd the most cost-eﬀective
solutions with PCMs for each building and climatic conditions, and to help decision-
making" (p. 98). Alam et al. (2014) found annual energy savings of 17-23% and
monthly averaged reduction in diurnal temperature swings of up to 4◦C when sim-
ulating a one-room house utilizing PCM-enhanced wallboards in 6 diﬀerent climate
zones of Australia.
The International Energy Agency, Energy Conservation through Energy Storage
(IEA-ECES) points out in their recent Annex 23 (Haghighat et al., 2013) that one
major obstacle for PCM-enhanced products to be adopted in buildings, is the lack
of simpliﬁed and easy accessible tools to predict, in a reliable way, the performance
and energy savings in buildings utilizing PCM-enhanced products. Speciﬁcally, it is
pointed out that the conﬁdence in the vast amount of numerical models available is
limited, due to lack of experimental validation through standardized tests. A few of
such standardized benchmarks are proposed in IEA-ECES Annex 23.
==PAGE:15==
1.2. PRINCIPLE OF PCM-ENHANCED WALL- AND CEILING BOARDS
3
1.2
Principle of PCM-enhanced wall- and ceiling
boards
The board incorporating PCM (PCM-board) is in contact with the indoor thermal
environment through radiation and convection. The PCM-board should be as close
to the room air as possible to maximize the heat transfer rate.
During the day the room air is heated up by solar energy, occupancy, lighting
and electrical equipment, which would normally prompt the air conditioning system
to cool the indoor air. However by utilising the thermal mass of the building, in
this case the PCM-boards, the indoor air temperature can be controlled passively.
The PCM begins to melt when the room is heated to above the melting point of the
PCM. The melting process is an endothermic process which absorbs thermal energy
from the surroundings. This energy is termed as latent heat when the temperature
is within the phase change range, and it is termed as sensible heat when outside the
phase change range. The phase where the PCM is heated up is called the charging
phase.
During the night when the air temperature decreases, the PCM starts to solidify.
The solidiﬁcation is an exothermic process in which thermal energy is released to the
surroundings. The phase where the PCM is cooled down is called the discharging
phase.
For the PCM to work eﬀectively, it should be completely solidiﬁed at the begin-
ning of occupancy, in order to have maximum available heat absorption.
The charging phase happens by radiation and natural convection when the PCM
is incorporated in PCM-boards. The discharge can be accomplished in diﬀerent ways
depending on the available discharging time, internal load and ambient conditions.
If the available discharge time is relatively long and/or the ambient nocturnal air
temperature is low, the ambient air can be used to discharge the boards through
natural ventilation. This is termed passive discharging in this thesis.
If a greater discharging rate is required, the ventilation may be done by the
mechanical ventilation system, or chilled water may be circulated in tubes embedded
in the boards. These types of discharging are called active discharging, since energy
is required to condition and/or move the ﬂuid.
The advantage of passive discharging is that it is cheaper to install since it
relies on a simple system using natural cooling sources. The advantage of active
discharging is that it is less sensitive to the local climate, and higher discharging
rates may be acquired. In this thesis the main focus will be air discharging with
possibility for conditioned and forced air ﬂow.
1.3
Aim of this thesis
In order to aid the adaptation of PCM-enhanced products in the building sector, the
purpose of this master’s thesis is to create a simpliﬁed method for designing oﬃce
buildings utilizing PCM-enhanced products. Speciﬁcally ceiling boards containing
PCM will be investigated. The basis for the simpliﬁed method will be numerical
simulations, validated through experiments in a climate chamber as well as through
a standardized benchmark test, proposed in IEA-ECES Annex 23.
==PAGE:16==
4
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
The simpliﬁed method should aid the designer in determining whether PCM-
technology is suited for a particular building, taking into account important para-
meters like local climate and building occupancy. Furthermore it should provide
means to determine the amount of PCM needed, exposed surface area needed and
suggested phase change temperature range.
==PAGE:17==
Chapter 2
Thermal Properties and
Classiﬁcation of PCM
5
==PAGE:18==
6
CHAPTER 2. THERMAL PROPERTIES AND CLASSIFICATION OF PCM
2.1
Heat storage
Heat storage plays an important role in the indoor thermal environment and the
energy performance of buildings. Heat storage works by absorbing heat from the
room during the day, when the heat load is high and releasing it during late after-
noon and night, when the heat load is low. The heat storage suppresses the diurnal
temperature ﬂuctuation in buildings, by decreasing the maximum temperature and
increasing the minimum temperature (Cheng et al., 2005), the so-called peak shaving
eﬀect. A building with high heat storage has a more stable indoor thermal environ-
ment, which is less inﬂuenced by rapid changes in the heat loads. Additionally, the
heat storage can provide energy savings by shifting the cooling load to night hours,
where the eﬃciency of the cooling equipment is higher, energy prices are lower and
free cooling can be utilised more readily. There are two methods to store heat in
buildings, sensible and latent heat storage.
2.1.1
Sensible heat storage
Sensible heat storage is caused by accumulation of energy in an object leading to a
rise in temperature. There is little or no change in volume and pressure. One of the
main physical properties for sensible heat storage is the speciﬁc heat capacity of a
material, which describes the amount of energy needed to increase the temperature
by one kelvin per kg mass of the material. The energy change due to temperature
change in a object is given by equation 2.1.
Q = cp · m · ∆T
(2.1)
Where Q [J] is the energy absorbed/released, m [kg] is the mass of the object, cp
[J/kgK] is the speciﬁc heat capacity and ∆T [K] is the temperature change of the
object.
2.1.2
Latent heat storage
When a material is heated to a certain temperature it undergoes a phase transition
from a structured state to a more dispersed state e.g.
melting of a solid.
This
endothermic process absorbs energy to break the molecular bonds and the energy
is stored as latent energy in the material.
When the temperature is reduced to
the freezing point, the energy is released back into the environment through an
exothermic process and the material freezes into its original solid state. Equation
2.2 describes the energy change for an object that undergoes a temperature and
phase change.
Q = m · (cp,1 · (Tphase −T1) + am · ∆Hm + cp,2 · (T2 −Tphase))
(2.2)
Where T1 [K] is the initial temperature, T2 [K] is the end temperature, Tphase
[K] is the phase change temperature, am [-] is the fraction of material undergoing
phase change, ∆Hm [J/kg] is the latent heat of fusion, m [kg] is the mass of the
object, cp,1 and cp,2 [J/kgK] are the speciﬁc heat capacity before and after the phase
change, respectively.
In Figure 2.1, the theoretical energy storage of a material undergoing a phase
change is compared to the sensible heat storage of rock and water. Water has the
==PAGE:19==
2.1. HEAT STORAGE
7
highest speciﬁc heat capacity of the three with 4.182 kJ/kgK. For the span of a
typical thermal comfort zone of e.g. 20-27◦C, water has a sensible energy storage of
29.3 kJ/kg. The theoretical energy stored during the phase transition of a certain
PCM is 190 kJ/kg, which is 6.5 times higher than for water. The large energy storage
during phase transition opens possibilities for the development of new methods for
implementing thermal mass into light-weight buildings, since a much smaller amount
of PCM is needed to provide the same thermal mass as a concrete wall.

==PAGE:20==

CHAPTER 2. THERMAL PROPERTIES AND CLASSIFICATION OF PCM
2.2
PCM
In buildings, latent heat storage can be incorporated by adding PCM to a host
material. The phase change of interest is, most commonly, when the PCM changes
between solid and liquid state. The addition of a PCM gives rise to two crucial
considerations; which PCM to use with regards to thermal properties and phase
transition and how to contain the PCM, since leakage, ﬂammability and corrosion
can pose problems if the PCM is not properly contained.
2.2.1
Types of PCMs
All compounds have the capability of undergoing phase transition, but only a limited
number of compounds have a phase transition in a feasible temperature range for
application in buildings.
In Figure 2.3 is presented several types of compounds
with a phase transition in the range -80 to 900◦C, along with the typical range of
interest for buildings. Most of the compounds are useless in buildings due to either
a too low or too high temperature of phase transition. The phase transition occurs
over a temperature range rather than at a well-deﬁned temperature. The latent
enthalpy depends on whether the PCM is heated or cooled. This phenomenon is
called hysteresis, illustrated in Figure 2.2.

Figure 2.2: Diﬀerential scanning calorimetry of PCM, showing two diﬀerent curves
for melting and freezing; the so-called hysteresis eﬀect (Kośny, 2015, p. 168).
The most interesting organic compounds for use in buildings are paraﬃns, fatty
acids, ethylene glycol and sugar alcohol along with the inorganic compound salt
hydrates.
Organic
Paraﬃns
are a mineral wax/oil and are used in candle-making. Paraﬃn consists
of hydrocarbons with the general formula CnH2n+2. Paraﬃns with a higher value of
n have a higher melting point and molecular weight. The paraﬃns with n = 16, 17

and 18 have a melting point of 18, 22 and 28◦C and can be used for regulating the
indoor thermal environment. Paraﬃns are attractive because they have a relatively
high heat of fusion (170-268 kJ/kg), narrow enthalpy curves, no super cooling and
good melting congruency.
The primary drawbacks of paraﬃns are low thermal
conductivity and high ﬂammability with toxic combustion products (Kośny, 2015).
Fatty acids
have the characteristic formula CH3(CH22nCOOH).
Fatty acids
have good melting congruency, are chemically stable and non-toxic. The heat of
fusion for fatty acids (131-212 kJ/kg) is lower than that of paraﬃns. The main
drawbacks of fatty acids are that they are slightly corrosive and more expensive
to produce if not made from waste products. They can however be produced in a
sustainable manner by extraction from vegetables and animal sources. Fatty acids
have a slight tendency to supercooling, meaning that the PCM is cooled below its
freezing point (Rozanna et al., 2005; Kośny, 2015).
Polyethylene glycols (PEGs)
are described by the chemical formula C2nH4n +
2On+1. PEGs have a heat of fusion of 100-200 kJ/kg which is lower than the other
organic PCMs. PEGs have a congruent melting, are non-toxic, chemically stable
and experience no super cooling (Karaman et al., 2011).
Sugar alcohols
have the general formula of HOCH2(CHOH)nCH2OH. Sugar
alcohols have great potential for PCM application, since they have a higher heat
of fusion than paraﬃns and fatty acids. They are inhibited by their high melting
temperature of above 90◦C, but can be used for storage tanks and other high-
temperature applications.
==PAGE:22==
10 CHAPTER 2. THERMAL PROPERTIES AND CLASSIFICATION OF PCM
Inorganic
Salt hydrates
are the most commonly used inorganic PCM. They are non-toxic
and non-ﬂammable and have larger heat of fusion (up to 296 kJ/kg) than organic
PCMs. They exhibit drawbacks of moderately corrosiveness, volume change during
phase change, incongruent melting leading to an irreversible phase separation and
supercooling.
2.2.2
Containment
There are three methods of PCM containment currently being used; impregnation,
macro- and micro-encapsulation.
Beyond encapsulation of solid-liquid PCM, re-
search is carried out in solid-solid PCMs, where a solid changes the molecular struc-
ture and encapsulation would be unnecessary (Gu et al., 2010).
Impregnation
Historically, impregnation was the ﬁrst used method of containment. The PCM was
added directly to porous materials, such as gypsum and concrete by submergence
into liquid PCM. The PCM was absorbed due to capillary action. The addition
of PCM through impregnation is cheap and enables the conversion of non-PCM
building products (Pavlov, 2014). Since the PCM is not encapsulated, there is a
slow continuous leakage of PCM when in liquid phase. The leakage can become
a problem during the lifetime of the PCM element, but the immediate concern is
discolouration of the surface, which is aesthetically undesirable (Kośny, 2015).
Macro-encapsulation
The PCM is contained in pouches preventing leakage of the PCM. The size of the
pouches is in the magnitude of 1-10 cm (Kośny, 2015).
This method is a relat-
ively cheap way of containing PCM but also fragile, since breaches of the macro-
encapsulation can occur during construction and when the building is in operation.
Micro-encapsulation
Micro-encapsulation is the process of creating extremely small particles of PCM
contained in various types of material, with size in the range of 1 −100 µm. The
particles consist of an outer shell of an impermeable material and a core of PCM.
Micro-encapsulation can be carried out in many ways including in-situ polymeriz-
ation and interfacial polymerization (Zhao and Zhang, 2011).
The advantage of
micro-encapsulated PCM is that the risk of breaching a capsule is completely re-
moved. Unfortunately, this method is more expensive than the previous mentioned
methods.
2.2.3
PCM board market research
A market research was conducted to obtain data on prices and properties for PCM
products, Table 2.1.

CHAPTER 3. EXPERIMENTAL STUDY
A series of measurements have been carried out with two purposes. The ﬁrst
purpose was to determine the performance of PCM ceiling boards in an oﬃce en-
vironment. The second purpose was to create a base for validation of numerical
simulation models, which will be presented later.
The measurements have been
carried out in a test facility located on the campus of the Technical University of
Denmark in the city of Kongens Lyngby (55◦47’02.4”N 12◦31’19.2”E).
3.1
Experimental set-up
This section describes the test facility, equipment and sensors of the experimental
set-up. The facility is a climate chamber with a suspended ceiling made of PCM-
boards, a ventilation system using the plenum above the suspended ceiling for air
supply and a hydronic system with pipes embedded in the PCM-boards. The hy-
dronic system is connected to two water storage tanks and four solar collectors, but
the focus has been on what is going on inside the climate chamber.
3.1.1
Climate chamber
The location for the experiments is a laboratory hall containing multiple climate
chambers.
Climate chamber 6 was used in the experiments.
Its dimensions are
5.45 x 4.2 x 3.75 m with a ﬂoor area of 22.89 m2, see Figure 3.1 for drawings and
Appendix A for construction drawings. The ﬂoor is made from perforated metal
tiles, with underlying thin metal sheets, raised 0.55 m above the concrete slab. The
chamber has a suspended ceiling 2.7 m above the ﬂoor containing the PCM boards.
Since the climate chamber is located in a hall, it is not exposed to ambient weather
conditions. The hall temperature ﬂuctuates in the range 21-24◦C.
The envelope of the climate chamber is built up by self-supporting sandwich
elements, consisting of insulating polyurethane foam enclosed by two 2 mm metal
sheets. The insulation thickness is 80 mm in the walls and roof and 100 mm in the
ﬂoor. The climate chamber is standing on a concrete slab with thickness 200 mm,
subjected to unknown but mild conditions on the underside, which is assumed to
be similar to the conditions of the hall. The polyurethane foam has a density of 70
kg/m3, heat conductivity of 0.05 W/mK and a speciﬁc heat capacity of 1500 J/kgK.
The U-value is 0.56, 0.43 and 0.57 W/m2K for the walls, ﬂoor and roof respectively,
with boundary resistance coeﬃcients of 0.17, 0.21 and 0.14 m2K/W respectively.
The doors are 50 mm thick and assumed to be of the same materials as the walls,
with a U-value of 0.85 W/m2K.
The windows are double layered 4-16-4 mm insulated glazing units with atmo-
spheric air in the cavity and no low emissivity coating1. The calculated center U-
value is 2.73 W/m2K (Pilkington, n.d.), see Appendix B. The windows are covered
on the outside with 10 mm expanded polystyrene with thermal conductivity of 0.04
W/mK to decrease the heat exchange. The total U-value of the window, including
the polystyrene, is 1.62 W/m2K.
1The properties of the windows were visually assessed in part by inspecting the writing on the
spacer and in part by looking at the color of reﬂections of a ﬂame in the glass panes.

3.1.2
PCM ceiling boards
The PCM boards consist of a core layer of cardboard with a thickness of 5 mm
for structural support. On either side is a mixed layer with thickness of 10 mm

from BASF, Figure 3.2. The PCM boards are 125 x 62.5 cm and each supposedly2
contains 6 kg of PCM, corresponding to 44% of the total mass of 13.5 kg. The surface
area of one PCM board is 0.765 m2 with the pipe connection cut-aways subtracted
and the density is 706 kg/m3. Data on the thermal properties are presented in Table
3.1.

3.1.3
Technical installations
There are two cooling systems connected to the climate chamber, allowing for dif-
ferent methods to discharge the PCM boards.
The ventilation was a constant air volume system (CAV) and was constantly
in operation. The supply duct was connected to an air handling unit on the ﬂoor
beneath the climate chamber. The duct entered the climate chamber through the
ﬂoor and went vertically through the room along the wall to the plenum above the
suspended ceiling. The air was supplied to the oﬃce space by gaps between the PCM
boards and extracted by an outlet in one corner of the oﬃce. A ventilation rate of
15.9 l/s was needed to dilute building emission and an additional 7 l/s per occupant.
This corresponded with the ventilation requirements for indoor air quality Category
II according to DS/EN 15251 (CEN, 2007). The target supply temperature was
18◦C, which was lower than the PCM freezing temperature to ensure that the PCM
boards could be discharged. Two ﬂow rates were tested; Qhigh with a target ﬂow
rate of 30 l/s which was suﬃcient for two occupants, and a reduced ﬂow rate, Qlow
with a target of 17 l/s. The reduced ﬂow rate was originally a mistake, but the
results were kept nevertheless for three reasons:
• The actual ﬂow rate was less of a concern than what happens, when it is
changed.
• The results with the low ﬂow rate can be used just as well for validation of the
numerical model, which was the main purpose of the experiments.
==PAGE:30==
18
CHAPTER 3. EXPERIMENTAL STUDY
• The air quality was not part of the study and it was interesting to see if thermal
comfort can be achieved with lower ventilation rates.
The second cooling system utilized the embedded pipes in the PCM boards to
provide cooling through chilled water, which was tapped from a cold water storage
tank. The tank was supplied with chilled water from nocturnal radiative cooling
panels on the roof of the experimental facility and by the main chiller of DTU
Campus.
If discharging by water was enabled, the chiller was automatically turned on if
the time was between 20:00-06:00, if the average operative temperature was above
21◦C and if the average bottom surface temperature of the PCM boards was above
18◦C. It was not intuitive to set the time schedule of the chiller, since the time had
to be converted to binary coded decimals (BCD) and then back to decimal numbers,
so 20:00 becomes 536870912. A short guide was created to illustrate the process for
future users of climate chamber 6, see Appendix C
The hot water storage tank was a Metro Therm type 2242 U2 7A and the cold
water storage tank was a Therm type 2242 2, both with a volume of 0.75 m3. Four
south facing solar collectors were located on the roof on top of the hall, but these were
not used in the present experiment. A schematic of the water system is presented
in Figure F.1 and F.2 in Appendix D. For further details on the system, refer to
section 5.1.3 of Grossule (2015) where the system is described in-depth
3.1.4
Heat gains
Thermal dummies were used to simulate occupants. The dummies consisted of vent-
ilation ducts (thin-gauge metal), roughly shaped as a human, with a high-resistance
wire inside to generate heat. The dummies were connected to a transformer to ad-
just the power output. The minimum setting was 105-114 W which was higher than
the sensible heat output of 62 W (61.5 % of the total heat output (Jensen et al.,
2006)) for oﬃce work with an activity level of 1.1 Met and a skin surface area of
1.8 m2 (ASHRAE, 2013). The computers were simulated by metal boxes heated
by light bulbs on the inside. The boxes were given a power closest possible to 125
W corresponding to the power of a computer and a monitor (Jensen et al., 2006).
General lighting was provided by four light bulbs hanging from the ceiling with an
average power of 18 W each. Desk lamps provided a comfortable work-lighting of
500 lux. All internal heat gains are detailed in Table 3.2.
Table 3.2: Power output of thermal objects.

chamber, was used to simulate solar heat gain to the oﬃce. The power output could
be adjusted in steps of 25 W up to a maximum of 175 W. This modest power output

3.1.5
Sensors
Several sensors were located in the climate chamber and on the suspended PCM
ceilings. The layout of the ceiling sensors is presented in Figure 3.5.
Figure 3.5: Suspended ceiling layout with sensor location.
==PAGE:32==
20
CHAPTER 3. EXPERIMENTAL STUDY
16 PT1000 Class A surface temperature sensors were located on the top and bot-
tom surface of eight PCM ceiling boards. Three heat ﬂux sensors of type M55A were
located on three diﬀerent PCM ceiling boards. Two stands were used to measure
parameters related to occupant comfort and the room air temperature. Each stand
had four PT1000 Class A air temperature sensors placed at 0.1 m, 0.6 m, 1.1 m and
1.7 m and an operative temperature sensor at 0.6 m w. The sensors were placed
according to the recommendations in DS/EN ISO 7726 (ISO, 2001). The PT1000
Class A sensors have an accuracy of 0.17-0.21◦C in the range 10-30◦C (Kongsberg,
2004). The operative temperature sensors have an accuracy of ±0.3◦C in the range
10-40◦C and were built at DTU (Simone et al., 2013) according to DS/EN ISO 7726
(ISO, 2001). The accuracy of the heat ﬂux sensors are not known.
An air temperature sensor was located in the supply duct, approximately 1.5 m
from the inlet. It was suspected that this sensor was not correctly calibrated. The
sensor was moved to 10 cm before the inlet, along with the air temperature sensor
from stand 2 at 0.6 m, and a digital thermometer, in order to assess the accuracy.
It was found that the supply temperature sensor measured temperatures that were
0.35◦C too low, so the stand 2 sensor remained in the inlet duct throughout the
experiments, to measure the supply temperature.
The ventilation ﬂow rate was continuously measured by the ventilation system,
but measurements with a FC0510 Micro-manometer with an IRIS 200 oriﬁce was
conducted to verify the ﬂow rate. A temperature sensor measures the air temperat-
ure outside climate chamber 6, in the laboratory hall.

3.1.6
Experimental scenarios
A total of ﬁve diﬀerent scenarios have been carried out during April 2016, with the
general settings as described in the previous section. The room was cooled solely
by ventilation in scenario 1-3 and with the addition of chilled water in scenario 4-
5, Table 3.3. The ventilation was in operation at all times with a constant ﬂow
rate, and the water circulation was enabled for automatic control in the time period
20:00-06:00. The performance of each scenario was evaluated in terms of thermal
comfort of the occupants and latent energy utilisation.

3.2
Results
Some of the raw results from scenario 1-3 are collected in Appendix F. The data can
e.g. be used by others for veriﬁcation of numerical models.
There were some problems during the experiment. The ﬂow rate sensor in the
hydronic system was faulty, since it showed a ﬂow rate when all valves were closed.
This means that the exact experimental conditions are unknown in scenario 4-5.
The problems with the ventilation system are described in the following section, as
well as workarounds used during the experiments. Then it is brieﬂy described how
the convergence or steady state was decided. After that follows the results of the
experiments.
3.2.1
Ventilation
When the air ﬂow rate to the climate chamber is as low as 17-30 l/s, the chiller
cannot cool the air to 18◦C, even during April when the outdoor temperature was
below 10◦C during the experiments. The reason is either that the chiller is faulty
or that the supply air has to travel a long way through the facility, from the chiller
to the climate chamber.
This leads to a temperature increase of the supply air
along the duct. Another problem in controlling the supply temperature is that the
feedback sensor is 20 m upstream from the climate chamber, making it impossible
to have a steady supply temperature due to diurnal temperature ﬂuctuations in the
laboratory hall.
In order to minimize the impact of these problems, the ﬂow rate was increased
and the excess ﬂow was bled just before the room. As a result, the system delivered
a total ﬂow of 250 l/s. This was adjusted between the supply to the climate chamber
and bleeding by manually setting the valves according to ﬂow rates measured with
a micro-manometer. Afterwards, 30 manual readings on the micro-manometer were
==PAGE:35==
3.2. RESULTS
23
taken, with 0.5 hours interval, in order to have a more accurate measurement of the
ﬂow rate. Qlow was measured at 17.96 ± 0.29 l/s and Qhigh was 29.86 ± 0.32 l/s
both with 95% conﬁdence.
Even with the high bleeding strategy with a constant temperature set point
of 18◦C, the supply air temperature experienced diurnal variation. The resulting
ventilation supply temperature for all ﬁve scenarios are presented in Figure 3.8.
In scenario 1, 2, 4 and 5, the supply temperature followed the same trend; the
supply temperature was at a minimum of approximately 17.3◦C at 8:00, then it
increased until the peak of approximately 18.1◦C at 16:45. Scenario 3 (the only
scenario with Qhigh) had the highest mean supply temperature and it appeared to
be oscillating. This was caused by increasing the ventilation rate by lowering the
amount of bleeding. The oscillation indicates poor control of the supply temperature
by the system.

3.2.2
Convergence
Each experiment was stopped after a few days, when the ﬂuctuations in surface
temperature of the PCM and air temperature were considered converged. The av-
erage PCM surface temperature and the average air temperature were used in this
assessment, Figure 3.9.
The temperatures increased until the 14th of April, and then decreased slightly
during the 14th of April. The scenario was considered converged on the 13th of
April and the variation between the two ﬁnal days was ascribed to changes in the
ambient conditions and supply air temperature, which could not be controlled. The
14th of April was selected as the representative day for scenario 1, because it is the
last full day.
Similar plots used to assess convergence can be found for the remaining scenarios
in Appendix G.

3.2.3
Operative temperature
To evaluate the thermal comfort in the diﬀerent scenarios, the operative temperat-
ure, Figure 3.10a, should be in the range 20-26◦C in order to fulﬁl DS/EN 15251
comfort Category II (CEN, 2007).
In scenarios 1 and 4, with one occupant, the low ventilation rate was suﬃcient
to cool the room, but when the occupant load was increased to two occupants in
scenario 2, the low ventilation rate alone was incapable of maintaining a comfortable
thermal environment. The operative temperature exceeded the threshold of 26◦C in
the period 13:00-17:45. The low ventilation rate was not suﬃcient to discharge the
PCM ceiling boards. If either the ventilation rate was increased to the high setting
(scenario 3) or chilled water was supplied to the ceiling (scenario 5), the cooling
eﬀect was large enough to handle two occupants. Scenario 5 only slightly exceeded
26◦C for one hour.
3.2.4
Vertical air temperature diﬀerence
The vertical air temperature diﬀerence between 0.1 m (ankle height) and 1.1 m (head
height for a seated person), Figure 3.10b, should not exceed 3◦C in order to fulﬁl
comfort Category A, according to DS/EN ISO 7730 (ISO, 2006). The requirement
was fulﬁlled in all scenarios.
The vertical air temperature diﬀerence was primarily inﬂuenced by the occupant
load. Scenario 1 and 4 with one occupant had a vertical air temperature diﬀerence
of 1◦C and scenario 2, 3 and 5 with two occupants had a diﬀerence of 1.6◦C. The
vertical air temperature diﬀerence was largest in the morning when the PCM ceiling
was discharged and the heat load was turned on. Around 14:30, when the latent
energy of the PCM boards was used, the vertical air temperature diﬀerence started
to decrease.

3.2.5
Utilisation of latent energy
To estimate how much of the potential latent energy was utilised in each scenario,
the minimum and maximum temperature of the PCM boards were used to calculate
the accumulated energy, using the eﬀective heat capacity curve of the PCM, Figure
3.3. The total latent energy of the PCM ceiling is 0.56 kWh, equation 3.2.
Elat = mboards ·
Z T2
T1
(cp,eff(T) −cp,sensible) dT
(3.2)
Where T1 and T2 [◦C] are the temperature end points of the phase change range 18-
27◦C, cp,eff and cp,sensible [J/kgK] is the eﬀective and sensible speciﬁc heat capacity
of the PCM boards, respectively, and mboards [kg] is the total mass of the PCM
boards, 324 kg. The sensible speciﬁc heat capacity is approximately 1310 J/kgK.
For each scenario, the maximum top surface temperature and minimum bottom
surface temperature are used to determine the latent energy utilisation.
This is
a conservative estimate, because choosing these temperatures will in this experi-
mental set-up always yield the smallest temperature range (top surface temperature
is always lowest).
The energy savings by using PCM boards is quantiﬁed by an equivalent ventila-
tion ﬂow rate, which would be needed to provide roughly the same thermal environ-
ment, if there were no PCM ceiling. Equation 3.3 is used to calculate the equivalent
ventilation ﬂow for an 8-hour day, when the temperature diﬀerence between supply
and exhaust is 5 K.
qeq =
Elat
∆T · cp,air · ρair · t
(3.3)

[kg/m3] is the speciﬁc heat capacity and density of air, respectively, and t [s] is the
ventilation time period.

The latent energy was utilised the least in scenario 1 and 4 with one occupant.
Scenario 1 was still quite eﬃcient since the PCM temperature drift overlaps the
peak heat capacity of the PCM. Scenario 4 was quite ineﬃcient because the PCM
temperature drift was too small to fully make use of the PCM. A much better
utilisation was found for the two-occupant scenarios 2, 3 and 5, with utilisation ratios
above 85%. Scenario 5 had the best utilisation since the temperature could increase
during the day with the low ventilation rate, and the chilled water circulation was
very eﬀective to discharge the PCM during night time. A higher utilisation could
be realised with the ventilation discharge if it had been possible to increase the ﬂow
rate during the night.
3.2.6
Cooling from ventilation and PCM ceiling
The cooling power from the PCM boards was compared to the cooling from ventil-
ation, Figure 3.11. The cooling power from ventilation was calculated by equation
3.4 to express it as cooling power per ﬂoor area. The surface heat ﬂux measured
on the PCM ceiling boards was likewise converted to cooling power per ﬂoor area,
equation 3.5.
Φvent = ∆T · qair · cp,air · ρair
Afloor
(3.4)
ΦPCM = Φsensor · APCM
Afloor
(3.5)
Where Φvent [W/m2] is the cooling power from ventilation per ﬂoor area, ΦPCM
[W/m2] is the cooling power from the PCM boards per ﬂoor area, Φsensor [W/m2]
is the cooling power per area PCM board, ∆T [K] is the air temperature diﬀerence
between supply and exhaust, qair [m3/s] is the ventilation rate, cp,air [J/kgK] is the
speciﬁc heat capacity of air, ρair [kg/m3] is the density of air, Afloor [m2] is the ﬂoor
area and APCM [m2] is the PCM ceiling area.
In the scenarios with low ventilation rate the cooling power from ventilation was
close to 4.5 W/m2 during the night and increased to 6 and 9 W/m2 during the day
for one- and two-occupant load, respectively. The high ventilation rate had a much

higher peak cooling rate of 13 W/m2. The absorbed heat ﬂux in the PCM was

scenario 5 having the largest absorption. Scenario 3 had a higher cooling power from
the ventilation, so for that scenario the primary cooling method was ventilation.
3.3
Conclusion
In scenario 1, a ventilation rate of 17 l/s was enough to provide cooling for one
occupant. For two occupants, the PCM was not fully discharged at this ventilation
rate. Either the ventilation rate had to be increased to 30 l/s or discharging by
embedded pipes had to be utilised.
Overall, it was found that the PCM ceiling
was able to provide a thermal comfort Category II/A for up to two occupants with
the proper discharging strategy. A comparison with a reference scenario without
PCM ceiling was unfortunately not possible, since the ceiling layout in the climate
chamber could not be changed
The PCM boards utilised 97% of the available latent heat in scenario 5, when
using the embedded pipe system for discharging. In scenario 4 with one occupant,
cooling by embedded pipes used only 35% of the latent heat, because the discharging
was too strong, compared to the heat loads.
There were several limiting factor that might prevent realistic scenarios. The
ventilation system was a CAV system, preventing the use of a higher ventilation
rate during night time. Scenario 3 was mainly cooled by ventilation during working
hours. With night ventilation it would have been possible to discharge the PCM
boards and provide a lower ventilation cooling power during the day. The layout of
the ventilation control system was not optimal, which resulted in diurnal variation
of the supply air temperature and ﬂuctuations in supply temperature in scenario 5.
Finally, the PCM surface had started to crumble and the latent enthalpy was quite
low compared to other products on the market. Nevertheless, the results obtained in
these experiments can be used to validate the numerical model, which is presented
in the next chapter.

Chapter 4
Validation of Numerical PCM
Model

==PAGE:42==
30
CHAPTER 4. VALIDATION OF NUMERICAL PCM MODEL
This chapter describes the process followed in setting up numerical models to
simulate a room with PCM, and the steps taken to validate the models against
experimental data.
4.1
Choice of software package
There are a number of commercial building energy simulation packages capable of
simulating PCM-materials, the most used being ESP-r, EnergyPlus and TRNSYS,
which are highlighted for their versatility and reliability (Soares et al., 2013). In this
thesis TRNSYS 17 (Klein et al., 2010) will be used due to its popularity in research
which has resulted in many available PCM components. TRNSYS 17 is a component
based modular transient simulation package, where users have the possibility to
edit existing components (called Types) or write their own.
Researchers in the
ﬁeld of PCM have developed several PCM components each with strengths and
weaknesses. The components diﬀer in the way they solve the partial diﬀerential
equation governing the heat conduction and in the way they model the phase change.
The PCM components used in this thesis1 are Type313 and Type399, developed
by Dentel and Stephan (2010).
Type313/399 can be used to model both active
and passive wall and ceiling systems.
Separate enthalpy curves for melting and
freezing can be used, to take into account the hysteresis eﬀect, which is important
to include in order to predict correctly the thermal behaviour (Kuznik and Virgone,
2009). When going from heating to cooling, the switch is instantaneous between
the freezing and melting heat capacity curves. Type313/399 uses a Crank-Nicolson
ﬁnite diﬀerence method to solve the heat conduction equation, see e.g. Grossmann
et al. (2007), and it uses the eﬀective heat capacity method to model the phase
change, see e.g. Lamberg et al. (2004); Poirier and Salcudean (1988); Haghighat
et al. (2013).
4.1.1
Short introduction to TRNSYS 17
TRNSYS is a transient simulation package, which can be used to simulate a wide
range of transient systems. The TRNSYS package consists of three separate pro-
grams; Simulation Studio, TRNBuild and TRNEdit.
In Simulation Studio, the system is designed by adding components and by
specifying inter-component relations.
One of these components is the Multizone
Building (Type56), which models the heat balance of one or more thermal zones in
a building. Type56 uses the Coeﬃcient Transfer Function method (CTF) to solve the
transient conduction through walls. Type56 is added as a component in Simulation
Studio and is then set up in TRNBuild, where walls, windows, internal heat gains,
ventilation, heating and cooling are speciﬁed. Finally, the program TRNEdit can
be used to edit the simulation input ﬁle and set up parametric studies.
1In the beginning of the thesis, Type313 was used, but was later supplemented by the newer
Type399.
==PAGE:43==
4.2. VALIDATION OF PCM MODEL
31
4.2
Validation of PCM model
The Type313/399 component has been experimentally validated to some extent as
part of its development, but it should be tested further on a standardized benchmark
to increase the conﬁdence in the model. This is in line with the recommendation in
IEAE-ECES Annex 23 (Haghighat et al., 2013), where diﬀerent benchmarks were
suggested. One of these is the Microbat test cells by Kuznik and Virgone (2009),
described in the following section.
4.2.1
Microbat test cells
The Microbat test cells by Kuznik and Virgone (2009) is a two-cell experimental
study on the eﬀect of including PCM-enhanced wallboard in a room. The two test
cells are cubic with side length of 0.5 m and opaque walls.
The test cells were
placed in a climate chamber, where the air temperature could be controlled and
three scenarios were tested; scenario A was a step increase in temperature from
15 to 30◦C, Scenario B was a corresponding step decrease and scenario C was a
sinusoidal variation between 15 and 30◦C with period of 24 hours. The step increase
and decrease are performed over a one and three hour period.
The cell without PCM (referred to as NOPCM cell) has one wall of 2 mm alu-
minium (front wall) and the rest (normal wall) are insulated with 60 mm poly-
urethane with 2 mm aluminium outside and 5 mm cardboard inside. In the cell
with PCM (referred to as PCM cell), the cardboard layer of the vertical walls are
substituted with 5 mm PCM-wallboards. The aluminium is polished to have a low
longwave emissivity, so thermal radiation exchange by the external surfaces may
be neglected. The inside surfaces are all painted black to maximize radiative heat
exchange between the internal surfaces.
The PCM-wallboard used in the experiment is Dupont ENERGAIN R
⃝. The wall-
board is a 5 mm copolymer with 60% by weight of micro-encapsulated paraﬃn. The
eﬀective heat capacity of the composite wallboard was measured with Diﬀerential
Scanning Calorimetry (DSC), see e.g. Höhne et al. (1996), for both melting and
freezing, so that hysteresis can be taken into account. The heat capacity curves are
presented in Figure 4.1. On this ﬁgure is also presented the actual curves used in
the TRNSYS model, which had to be approximated, since Type313 can only accept
16 data points on each curve.
For more details on the Microbat experiments, please refer to the original paper
by Kuznik and Virgone (2009).
Set up of the model in TRNSYS
A multizone building project is created. This adds the necessary components in
the Simulation Studio and creates the links between them. The components for
weather, shading and lighting control are deleted, since these are not needed. The
components and connections can be seen in Figure 4.2.
In TRNBuild the three wall types are created.
The wall category is set to
"Boundary", with the boundary temperature set to the output of Type313 for PCM
walls and to the ambient conditions for the rest. The internal longwave emission
coeﬃcient is kept at the default value of 0.90, since a diﬀerent value would only be

313: PCM Walls
65c: Plot & Print
14e: Tamb
Figure 4.2: Simulation Studio with components and connections used for the Mi-
crobat benchmark.
considered if the detailed radiation model is used. Also, 0.90 is close to the value of a
black surface which would be 0.92. Longwave radiation of the external surface is not
included when the wall category is "Boundary" and there are no solar heat gains, so
the solar absorptance and the longwave emission coeﬃcient are just kept at default
values. The internal convective heat transfer coeﬃcient is kept at the default of 3.06
W/m2K for non-PCM walls, which is in good agreement with correlations found in
literature (David et al., 2011). Liu and Awbi (2009) found that a PCM-surface has a
higher convective heat transfer coeﬃcient than non-PCM surfaces due to increased
heat exchange so the coeﬃcient is set to 5 W/m2K for PCM-walls, which is close
to the value they found. The external convective heat transfer coeﬃcient is set to
0.0001 W/m2K, which in TRNSYS ensures perfect contact. This is important for
the PCM wall, since it should connect to the surface temperature calculated by the
==PAGE:45==
4.2. VALIDATION OF PCM MODEL
33
external Type313 without resistance. For the other walls it makes little physical
sense to have perfect contact between the surface and ambient air, but the default
coeﬃcient of 3.06 W/m2K results in a much slower heat transfer to the cells.
Because of the quite big diﬀerence in thermal diﬀusivity of the normal wall and
front wall, the program has trouble calculating the coeﬃcients of the CTF-method.
The front wall, with its very high thermal diﬀusivity, requires a very small timebase
(< 0.0001) to calculate the CTF coeﬃcients, whereas the normal wall requires a
larger timebase (> 0.001). This was solved by substituting the front wall with an
equivalent resistance massless layer, with no thickness, eﬀectively disregarding the
thermal capacity of the 2 mm aluminium plate, which is a good approximation.
The PCM wall is set as a massless layer with very low thermal resistance (< 0.0001
m2K/W), since this wall type is described by the external component, Type313.
The layers constituting the PCM-wall are set up in Type313. There is a restric-
tion that the PCM layer has to be the second layer, so a ﬁctive ﬁrst layer is created
with very low density and thermal capacity and very high thermal conductivity.
Type313 is connected to the multizone building component (Type56) in Simula-
tion Studio. Type313 calculates the surface temperatures and is given Tstar as the
boundary temperature from the Type56 thermal zone. Tstar is a ﬁctive temperature
that takes into account the convective and radiative heat transfer. An associated
resistance Requiv from Type56 is used as the surface resistance. The ambient tem-
perature is connected to the external side with a convective heat transfer coeﬃcient
of 3.06 W/m2K.
The ambient temperature is described by discrete points speciﬁed in the forcing
function (Type14e). This Type interpolates between the deﬁned points.
Results of Microbat model
The results of the simulations are shown in Figure 4.3 for the step changes and
in Figure 4.4 for the sinusoidal variation.
For the NOPCM cell, the simulation
predicts accurately the air temperature in all scenarios, with Root Mean Squared
Errors (RMSE) in the range [0.14, 0.34], with the largest errors for the cooling and
sinusoidal scenarios. For the PCM cell there is in general some discrepancy between
experiment and simulation. The discrepancy is small during heating (RMSE = [0.28,
0.29]), with a satisfactory prediction, but during cooling the discrepancy cannot be
neglected (RMSE = [0.71, 0.81]). The rate of temperature change does not seem to
have a clear eﬀect on the accuracy of prediction. For all scenarios it is observed that
cooling is diﬃcult to predict, especially when PCM is included in the simulation.
In the cooling scenarios there is a 1 hour plateau around 19◦C which is not
predicted by the simulation.
The plateau is believed to be due to how paraﬃn
solidiﬁes in a micro-capsule. The paraﬃn solidiﬁes from the outside, reducing the
amount of liquid paraﬃn that can transfer heat by convection, reducing the heat
transfer rate (Kuznik et al., 2010). This detailed process is simply not implemented
in the current PCM components.

4.2.2
Climate chamber
The results of the climate chamber experiments for scenario 1-3, presented in section
3.1 and 3.2, are used in this section to validate the PCM simulation model. This
extra validation is done to test the model in a more complex case than the Microbat
validation described in the previous section. Scenario 4-5, the cases utilising the
embedded pipe system, are not modelled, since the ﬂow rate sensor in the water
circuit was faulty.
Set up of the model in TRNSYS
Type399, which is the successor of Type313, is used in this section. The numerical
scheme is exactly the same and the two Types give the same results.
The new
feature of Type399 is the possibility to place the PCM layer anywhere in the wall,
thus avoiding having a ﬁctive layer, and the boundary conditions are more well-
described. The components and connections can be seen in Figure 4.5.
56: Building
14a: Occupancy
9a: Solar panel
399: PCM Ceiling
Eq1 (htc)
Units
9a: Exp logged
65c: Plot & Print
Figure 4.5: Simulation Studio with components and connections used to simulate
the climate chamber.
A more detailed schematic showing the connections between Type56 and Type313/399
can be seen in Figure 4.6 for an internal wall using the standard (non-geometrical)
longwave radiation model.
The climate chamber is modelled in TRNSYS 17 in a similar way to the Mi-
crobat test cell, however now the Type56 contains three thermal zones: Underﬂoor,
Chamber and Plenum. The geometry is according to Figure 3.1. All outer walls are
wall type "Boundary" with the logged temperature of the laboratory air as the pre-
scribed boundary temperature. The lower thermal resistance of doors and windows
is taken into account by reducing the thermal resistance of the respective walls, by
calculating an area weighted thermal resistance, Req, by equation 4.1.
==PAGE:49==

Where Rs [m2K/W] is the thermal resistance between the outer surfaces of a struc-
ture (wall, window or door), A [m2] is the corresponding area and RSBR [m2K/W]
is the surface boundary resistance, which for this calculation is set to 0.17 m2K/W.
Two walls with reduced thermal resistance are calculated. One wall with 4.2 m2
doors and 2 m2 windows having a reduced U-value of 0.79 W/m2K and the other
wall with 1.76 m2 door and a 1 m2 window having a reduced U-value of 0.70 W/m2K.
The U-value of a wall without reductions is 0.56 W/m2K. All U-values are given here
with a surface boundary resistance of 0.17 m2K/W.
Non-PCM surfaces are given the default heat transfer coeﬃcient of 3.06 W/m2K
on internal and external surfaces. The PCM ceiling has a heat transfer coeﬃcient
of 5.0 W/m2K as in the Microbat simulation.
The internal ﬂoor between the Chamber and Underﬂoor zones consists of thin
steel sheets with perforated steel tiles on top. It is assumed that the thermal resist-
ance is equal to the surface boundary resistance.
The ceiling is divided into a PCM ceiling zone with area of 18.37 m2, and a
non-PCM ceiling zone of 4.52 m2 consisting of 10 mm insulating foam plate. The
PCM ceiling is created with a very low thermal resistance massless dummy layer,
and the boundary surface temperatures on the Chamber side and the Plenum side
are loaded in externally from Type399. Only one cp-curve for melting is available
from the guarded hot plate test, so hysteresis cannot be taken into account in this
validation, Figure 3.3 on page 17.
The ventilation is on at all times and assumed constant with a mass ﬂow rate
of 77.85 kg/h (17.96 l/s) or 129.43 kg/h (29.86 l/s) and the temperature follows
the logged ventilation air temperature from the experiment. The ventilation air is
supplied to the Plenum thermal zone and a coupling air ﬂow is set up from the
Plenum zone to the Chamber zone, simulating the ventilation air passing through
the PCM ceiling boards.
The internal heat gains are speciﬁed according to the experimental scenarios.
==PAGE:50==
38
CHAPTER 4. VALIDATION OF NUMERICAL PCM MODEL
The distribution between radiative and convective heat is set according to the
TRNSYS manual (Klein, 2014). The heat gains from occupants, computers and
the heating panel are 16.7% radiative and the heat gains from lighting is 66.7%
radiative.
The logged temperatures and heat ﬂuxes from the experiments are read into the
Simulation Studio by the Type9a data reader. The experimental data are provided
at a time step of 0.25 hours, except for the solar heating panel, which is for every
0.5 hours. The simulation time step is set to 0.01 hours and the timebase to 0.05
hours, which ensures a smooth curve with no visible steps in the output.
Type399 can only contain one PCM layer, hence the actual ceiling board with
PCM-cardboard-PCM conﬁguration cannot be modelled. The simpliﬁcation used
here is to approximate the ceiling board by one homogeneous layer. The thermal
conductivity and speciﬁc heat capacity are taken from the guarded hot plate test,
as described in section 3.1.2. The material data used in the simulation is presented
in Table 4.1.

Results of climate chamber model
Several consecutive days are simulated, but only the representative day, from each
experimental scenario, is presented here. In Figure 4.7 and 4.8 the measured and
simulated values are compared. The chamber air temperature, bottom surface tem-
perature of the PCM-ceiling boards and the heat ﬂux from the bottom surface are
presented.
The measured values of heat ﬂux and surface temperature are taken as the
average over all sensors. The air temperature is calculated by interpolating between
sensor height 1.1 m and 1.7 m to the vertical middle of the room in height 1.35 m,
and then averaged between the two air temperature sensor stands.
The prediction of air and surface temperature is considered accurate (RMSE =
[0.19, 0.34]). Both the temperatures are overestimated by the simulation during the
cooling phase around 20-24:00. The biggest errors occur in the cooling phase, as
was also the case with the Microbat validation. Here the discrepancy is maximum
0.7◦C.
The simulation overestimates the heat ﬂux quite substantially during the occu-
pancy hours (RMSE = [1.29, 1.81]). A slight underestimation is observed during
the late evening. There was a problem with obtaining a good adherence between
==PAGE:51==
4.2. VALIDATION OF PCM MODEL
39
the heat ﬂux sensors and the ceiling boards during the experiments, because the
clay had started to crumble, making the surface non-smooth. This may have cause
the measured heat ﬂuxes to be less than they actually were and can explain the
discrepancy.

CHAPTER 4. VALIDATION OF NUMERICAL PCM MODEL
4.2.3
Conclusion on validation
The simulation model has been validated against two diﬀerent experimental set-ups;
the climate chamber 6 experiments with a suspended PCM ceiling, and the Microbat
test cells by Kuznik and Virgone (2009).
While the validation did not yield acceptable accuracy all the time, the process
itself revealed many initial errors that has been corrected and it gave a deeper
understanding of TRNSYS and Type56 as well as the strengths and limitations of
Type313/399.
During the simulation of the Microbat test cells it was found that Type313/399
has a maximum number of 16 data points for specifying each of the cp-curves.
This was not apparent from the documentation, and going beyond this limit means
that the simulation uses an incorrect eﬀective heat capacity. It was also evident
that setting the melting and freezing temperatures correctly in the parameters of
Type313/399 is important for the model to choose the correct cp-curve and for
deciding when to switch between the two curves. These errors would have been very
hard to detect, if a longer period or more complex model was simulated right away.
Both validations show higher discrepancy during cooling than during heating.
For the in-house climate chamber validation, this discrepancy might be a con-
sequence of using only a melting cp-curve and thus not taking hysteresis into account.
The heat ﬂux was overestimated by the simulation. This might be due to insuﬃcient
contact between the heat ﬂux sensors and the PCM ceiling boards, which was noted
during the experiments. Full year simulations can now be carried out with bigger
conﬁdence in the validity of the model, keeping in mind the shortcomings mentioned
in this chapter.
==PAGE:55==
Chapter 5
Parametric Study
43
==PAGE:56==
44
CHAPTER 5. PARAMETRIC STUDY
Soares et al. (2013) concludes in their extensive review that numerical studies are
typically focused on extreme conditions of summer or winter and only few studies
for autumn and spring exist.
In this chapter is presented a whole year numerical parametric study. The idea
of the parametric study is to simulate diﬀerent conﬁgurations of a common oﬃce
building, located in representative and relevant climatic zones. Designers can use
the results of the parametric study to help determine if PCM technology is a viable
energy-saving solution in a particular design case.
One important thing to consider is that often, the parameters cannot be optim-
ised individually, since the setting of one parameter inﬂuences the optimal setting
of other parameters.
This complex relationship between parameters necessitates
simultaneous optimization, which can be achieved by simulating all combinations.
5.1
Relevant parameters
There are many parameters to consider when designing a building utilising PCM-
enhanced building components like wall- and ceiling boards. Such relevant para-
meters have been identiﬁed and discussed in the literature (Tzivanidis et al., 2012;
Soares et al., 2013; Koo et al., 2011). The identiﬁed parameters will be discussed in
the following sections.
5.1.1
Latent enthalpy
The latent enthalpy is the amount of energy stored and released during the phase
change. A higher latent enthalpy means that more energy can be absorbed and re-
leased, thus increasing the eﬀect of the PCM-enhanced product. The latent enthalpy
varies between diﬀerent pure PCMs. The latent heat of a PCM-enhanced product
can be increased by increasing the PCM content in the product. However, there are
practical limitations on the PCM content that can be attained, which depends on
the host material.
A higher latent heat per volume is always desirable from an energy point of view
(Al-Saadi and Zhai, 2015), but the price of the product may increase as well. When
considering latent heat per area of e.g.
PCM-enhanced ceiling boards, a higher
latent heat can also be acquired by choosing a thicker ceiling board, but this may
aﬀect the thermal performance negatively, see section 5.1.7.
See section 5.1.4 for the chosen levels of this parameter.
5.1.2
Phase change temperatures
This is the temperature where the PCM changes phase between solid and liquid.
To fully utilize the PCM, it has to go through at least one complete melting and
solidiﬁcation cycle per day. This only happens if the PCM temperature is lower than
the solidiﬁcation temperature in the morning and higher than melting temperature
in the afternoon. The melting and freezing point of a particular PCM normally do
not coincide, thus creating a hysteresis eﬀect.
Alam et al. (2014) found that the optimal phase change temperature varies
with the climate zone, where warmer climates beneﬁt from a higher phase change
temperature. The optimal phase change temperature also changes throughout the
==PAGE:57==
5.1. RELEVANT PARAMETERS
45
year because of changes in the weather conditions. For this reason the choice of
phase change temperature in a design situation should be made based on a full year
energy simulation.
Other authors have found that the phase change temperature should be close
to the room temperature set point, which may diﬀer between geographic locations.
Warmer climates beneﬁts from higher melting temperature (Medved and Arkar,
2008). Alam et al. (2014) concludes that the phase change has to be within the
thermal comfort range to save energy, i.e.
it should be ensured that the daily
temperature ﬂuctuation exceeds the phase change temperature both in melting and
solidiﬁcation.
See section 5.1.4 for the chosen levels of this parameter.
5.1.3
Phase change temperature range
Koo et al. (2011) notes that the phase change temperature range (PCTR) should
be as small as possible. A small PCTR means that the temperature ﬂuctuation can
be allowed smaller, which may improve the indoor thermal environment. A small
PCTR can be hard to obtain though, since it is a consequence when mixing diﬀerent
PCMs, which is done to reach a desired average phase change temperature.
Other authors found beneﬁts when the PCTR is large, probably because the
PCM is activated during a longer period of the year (Medved and Arkar, 2008).
See section 5.1.4 for the chosen levels of this parameter.
5.1.4
Eﬀective heat capacity curves
The cp-curve is the thermal characteristic of the PCM-enhanced product. It de-
scribes the eﬀective heat capacity as a function of temperature. A market research
revealed several products with diﬀerent melting/freezing temperatures, PCTR and
latent heat. It is desired to generalize these products, so other products with similar
characteristics could be used with the simpliﬁed design method. One way to do this
is to describe the cp-curve with a mathematical function, see equation 5.1, adapted
from Medved and Arkar (2008).
cp,eff (TPCM) =

(5.1)
Where the parameters α, β and γ [-] are constants to shape the curve, Tp [◦C] is the
temperature corresponding to the peak cp-value cp,eff,peak [J/gK], Tm,end [◦C] is the
temperature at the end of melting, TPCM [◦C] is the PCM temperature and cp,sens
[J/gK] is the speciﬁc sensible heat capacity.
The cp-curve presented in Figure 5.1 has been generated using the Matlab script
in Appendix H based on equation 5.1. The Matlab script ﬁnds the correct curve
shape parameters by continuously determining the error on the latent heat by nu-
merical integration and on the melting start temperature by diﬀerentiation. The
melting start temperature is deﬁned as the intersection between the tangent to the

curve spans 2 K.

5.1.5
Thermal conductivity
The thermal conductivity of the PCM-enhanced product is important, since it aﬀects
how fast the PCM can absorb and release energy from the room. A high thermal
conductivity is always desired, and increasing the thermal conductivity is a subject
of interest for researchers. High-conductivity materials such as aluminium powder,
metal oxide and expanded graphite can be used to enhance the thermal conductivity
(Kośny, 2015). Typical values for thermal conductivity are 0.2 W/mK for paraﬃn
and 0.5 W/mK for salt hydrates without any enhancements.
Two diﬀerent thermal conductivities are simulated; 0.20 and 0.40 W/mK. The
low setting may be a PCM product with paraﬃn and the high setting may either
be salt hydrate or a paraﬃn with enhanced conductivity.
5.1.6
PCM amount
If the amount of PCM is too high it might not be solidiﬁed completely during the
available night cooling time, or may not be completely melted during the day time
heat absorption, or both (Soares et al., 2013). Insuﬃcient quantity of PCM results
in complete melting and solidiﬁcation with extra unused discharging capacity during
night. This would result in overheating of the room, since there would be excess
heat that the PCM cannot absorb.
There is a limit to how much PCM can be incorporated into a product. For
micro-encapsulated paraﬃn in gypsum this limit is around 30% by weight. Higher
percentages can be attained in some composite materials, where a PCM content of
up to 60% by weight has been obtained (Kośny, 2015; Kuznik et al., 2008).
==PAGE:60==
48
CHAPTER 5. PARAMETRIC STUDY
5.1.7
PCM layer thickness
Kuznik et al. (2008) showed that the optimum thickness of a PCM-enhanced wall
board on an internal partition wall is 1 cm. The optimum thickness changes with the
combined heat transfer coeﬃcient and thermal conductivity. A higher heat transfer
coeﬃcient and thermal conductivity allow for a bigger thickness and larger energy
storage. If a thick PCM board has a low thermal conductivity, then the transient
penetration depth might not be large enough to activate the whole thickness. Koo
et al. (2011) found that there is a critical thickness which gives the highest energy
storage, after which the stored energy decreases slightly. A thickness in the range
10-30 mm is optimal for combined heat transfer coeﬃcients of 3-12 W/m2K (Koo
et al., 2011).
Two diﬀerent PCM board thickness are used in the parametric study; 10 mm
and 20 mm .
5.1.8
Climatic conditions
An important design parameter for the performance of passive thermal mass util-
isation is the diurnal temperature range (DTR), which is the temperature diﬀerence
between day and night. A DTR greater than 12-15 ◦C is optimal for free cooling
systems (Raj and Velraj, 2010; Waqas and Ud Din, 2013). The free cooling poten-
tial is positively correlated with a higher DTR and the average ambient temperature
has only minor inﬂuence on the cooling potential (Medved and Arkar, 2008; Takeda
et al., 2004). Shaviv et al. (2001) found a linear correlation between the reduction
of the maximum indoor temperature and the DTR for the hot-humid Israeli cli-
mate. Alam et al. (2014) simulated a one-room house in six diﬀerent climate zones
of Australia and found that the potential for energy savings is diﬀerent. The hot
and humid climate of Darwin, Australia, did not have potential for energy savings,
whereas cold, mild and hot temperate climates could save energy with PCM.
Six cities have been chosen for the parametric study. The chosen cities have
available weather data including solar data, Figure 5.2, are placed in medium to
high population density areas, Figure 5.3 and represent diﬀerent climate zones and
thus have diﬀerent mean temperature and DTR, Figure 5.4. The climate map has
been generated using the open source geographic information system (GIS) software
QGIS (QGIS Development Team, 2016) with climate data from International Water
Management Institute (n.d.) and Hijmans et al. (n.d.). Other climate maps can be
found in Appendix J.
The chosen locations are shown in Figure 5.5 and details are presented in Table
5.2.

5.1.9
Room temperature set points
Requirements for thermal comfort vary from country to country depending on the
local climate. To determine thermal comfort ranges for the diﬀerent climate zones,
the adaptive method from DS/EN 15251 (CEN, 2007) is used. The thermal comfort
range is calculated based on the running mean outdoor temperature (Φrm) which is
calculated by equation 5.2.
Φrm = (1 −α) · Φed−1 + α · Φrm−1
(5.2)
Where Φed−1 [◦C] is the mean temperature of the previous day, Φrm−1 [◦C] is the
running mean of the previous day and α is a damping constant between 0 and 1,
suggested at 0.8.
The adaptive method is limited to buildings without mechanical cooling and
heating, but night ventilation is permissible. The method is still used even though
the building has a cooling and heating system, since it is the best approach to
quantify thermal comfort in diﬀerent climates.
The lower and upper limits for thermal comfort Category III are calculated ac-
cording to DS/EN 15251, Annex A (CEN, 2007), using weather ﬁles from TRNSYS.
The thermal comfort range for each climate zone is presented in Table 5.3.
Table 5.3: Thermal comfort range for the diﬀerent climate zones.
AR

5.1.10
Night cooling duration
If the ambient air is used to discharge the PCM, then a high ventilation rate and
low temperature is beneﬁcial, but this would also increase the risk of discomfort
for occupants. For this reason, the discharging phase should be commenced outside
occupancy hours. When there is suﬃcient available time for discharging the PCM,
the ventilation rate can be lower and free cooling can be utilized more readily.
In the parametric study, discharging is allowed when the occupants are not
present, i.e. from 16:00-08:00, and when the ambient air temperature permits it.
This is described in more detail in the controls section 5.2.6.
5.1.11
Temperature of ventilation air
The temperature of the ventilation air aﬀects the discharge rate of the PCM. In
practice the temperature may be constricted in free cooling scenarios where the
outside air is utilized in the discharging phase, which is the case in the parametric
study in this thesis. The ambient air may need some heat from either a heating coil
or the heat exchanger, if there is a risk for condensation in the room, i.e. the supply
temperature is lower than the room dew point. This is described in more detail in
the controls section 5.2.6.
==PAGE:64==
52
CHAPTER 5. PARAMETRIC STUDY
5.1.12
Internal heat gains
The internal heat gains from occupants, lighting and electrical equipment have an
impact on the performance of a PCM system. High heat gains will quickly heat up
the room air, which will put a high demand on the heat transfer rate into the PCM
in order to store the excess heat. A high internal heat gain may beneﬁt from a lar-
ger exposed surface area of the PCM product, higher thermal conductivity, higher
convective heat transfer coeﬃcient and larger latent heat capacity. The distribu-
tion of the heat sources in the room is also important to consider. Concentrated
heat sources aﬀects mainly the PCM boards close by, while a uniform heat source
distribution aﬀect all PCM in the room, leading to a better utilisation of the PCM.
In the parametric study, two occupants are present in each oﬃce. The activity
level is assumed to be "seated, light working" (ISO, 2006), which results in a sensible
and latent heat of 75 W. The lighting is on when the occupants are present 08:00-
16:00 with a constant power of 53 W in the oﬃce and 18 W in the hallway, of which
66% is radiative. Electrical equipment has two power levels, 150 W and 300 W
in each oﬃce, of which 29% is radiative. The distribution between radiative and
convective heat is according to Jensen et al. (2006). The power per area in each
oﬃce is 16.8 W/m2 with 150 W equipment and 24.0 W/m2 with 300 W equipment.
5.1.13
Heat transfer coeﬃcient
The heat transfer coeﬃcient should be as high as possible to facilitate a high energy
transfer between the PCM, the room air and the surfaces. However, a high convective
heat transfer coeﬃcient means a high air velocity, which may increase the risk of
draught, if the air velocity is also elevated in the occupancy zone.
David et al. (2011) studied three diﬀerent correlations for natural convection and
obtained average convective heat transfer coeﬃcients of 1.37, 1.81 and 3.87 W/m2K,
depending on which formulation is used. They also studied a correlation for mixed
convection and found coeﬃcients in the range 1.29-1.5 W/m2K for low velocities
(0-0.20 m/s) and in the range 2.4-4.6 W/m2K for higher velocities (0.40-1.0 m/s).
By numerical simulation they concluded that the convective heat transfer coeﬃcient
has a very large impact on the energy stored in the PCM layer, speciﬁcally it was
found that the beneﬁt of having a PCM layer more than doubled when going from
1.37 to 3.86 W/m2K.
Al-Saadi and Zhai (2015) used a constant internal convective heat transfer coef-
ﬁcient of 4.43 W/m2K, based on experiments by Liu and Awbi (2009), who also
found that convective heat transfer coeﬃcients for PCM walls are underestimated
when using correlations for normal walls.
In the parametric study, a constant heat transfer coeﬃcient of 3.06 W/m2K is
used for non-PCM surfaces and 5.0 W/m2K for PCM surfaces, since these values
gave good results in the validation models.

Geometry and properties
A section of an oﬃce building, which will be used in the simulations, is presented

Figure 5.6: 3D-model of oﬃce building section with two oﬃces connected through a
hallway.
Each oﬃce has a number of windows, simulated as one window described by the
equivalent glazing area (EGA), equation 5.3.
EGA = Ag
Af
· gg
(5.3)
Where Ag is the glazing area, Af is the ﬂoor area and gg is the solar energy trans-
mission coeﬃcient of the glazing. The window is varied in size, 5.25 m2 and 7.88
m2, corresponding to a glazing to ﬂoor ratio of 21% and 33% and a EGA-value of
0.059 and 0.092.
A constant inﬁltration rate is used for all climate zones. This is a simpliﬁcation
that enables the model to run faster, by not calculating the wind-induced inﬁltration
at each time step. This is a good approximation when looking at whole-year energy
==PAGE:66==
54
CHAPTER 5. PARAMETRIC STUDY
use, especially when simulating modern, air tight buildings. The constant inﬁltration
rate can be estimated by the K-P model by Kronvall and Persily, mentioned by
Sherman (1987), which estimates the inﬁltration rate based on the leakage at 50
Pa overpressure. The Danish Building Regulations prescribe a maximum allowed
leakage at 50 Pa overpressure of 1.0 l/s/m2 ﬂoor area for building class 2015 (The
Danish Transport and Construction Agency, 2015).
This is converted to an air
change rate at natural conditions, equation 5.4.
ACHK−P =
q50
N · hroom
· 3.6
(5.4)
Where q50 [l/s/m2] is the leakage at 50 Pa overpressure, hroom [m] is the room height,
3.6 converts from l/s to m3/h and N is the K-P model conversion factor, which is
20. This results in a constant inﬁltration rate of 0.055 h−1

5.2.2
Exterior walls
A well-insulated wall is important in all climates. In cold climates, a well-insulated
wall minimizes heat losses during winter and in hot climates, the wall keeps the
conditioned air contained inside the building.
A light-weight exterior wall is used so the PCM ceiling is the main contributor to
the thermal mass of the room. The wall is built up by 8 mm ﬁbre cement plates as
the external surface, a ventilated air cavity, 315 mm insulation and gypsum boards
as the inside surface. A structural steel skeleton placed in the insulation provides the
wall with load bearing capacity. The wall has a U-value of 0.11 W/m2K including
a surface boundary resistance (SBR) of 0.17 m2K/W. A sketch of the external wall
is presented in Figure 5.7.
5.2.3
Windows
A window with solar control coating is required, since it is the only method of solar
shading in the model. Pilkington’s 3-layered argon-ﬁlled glazing, 6C(66)-18Ar-4-
18Ar-S(3), provides a low g-value of 0.33, while maintaining a suﬃcient light trans-
mittance of 0.61 and a U-value of 0.50 W/m2K, see Appendix K for the data sheet.
