5.2.4
Internal walls, ﬂoor and ceiling
The internal walls consist of two layers of gypsum drywall (26 mm) on either side
of 50 mm insulation. This is a partition wall with a low thermal capacity and a
U-value of 0.54 W/m2K including a surface boundary resistance of 0.13 m2K/W on
both surfaces.
==PAGE:68==
56
CHAPTER 5. PARAMETRIC STUDY
The ﬂoor slab is a 240 mm reinforced concrete plate. The suspended ceiling is
made of gypsum drywall material with a thickness of 20 mm.
5.2.5
Heating, ventilation and air conditioning
Each oﬃce has a heating and cooling system with set points as presented in Table
5.3. The heating and cooling system will always maintain a comfortable thermal
environment.
Therefore, the eﬀect of the PCM ceiling will be quantiﬁed by the
impact on energy use of the heater and cooler rather than improved indoor thermal
environment.
The ventilation system contains a heat exchanger that can preheat or precool
the supply air. A controller regulates the return ﬂow through the heat exchanger to
condition the supply temperature. The same ventilation air temperature is supplied
to both oﬃces. The supply temperature varies between the ambient temperature,
when the heat exchanger is bypassed, and the maximum heat recovery temperat-
ure. A central heating coil can be used to heat the air further, if there is risk of
condensation in one or both oﬃces.
The ﬂow rate can be regulated independently to each oﬃce. The ﬂow rate can
be either 14.7 l/s, 28.7 or 60 l/s, depending on whether occupants are present and
if discharging of the PCM is needed. This is described in detail in the following
section. The ventilation rates for occupancy and building emissions were calculated
according to DS/EN 15251 (CEN, 2007). The ventilation system operates with a
set point range that is 2◦C narrower than the comfort range, in order to utilise free
heating and cooling before the room heater and cooler are switched on.
5.2.6
Description of the control system
A rather complex control logic has been set up to eﬃciently use the potential for
free cooling to discharge the PCM ceiling during night time in the cooling season.
The control logic diagrams were created with the free and open source software Dia
(Larsson et al., 2011).
During cooling season, the PCM should be discharged during night time to have
available latent capacity for the next day. However, during the heating season, the
stored heat should instead be kept inside the building to minimize energy consump-
tion for room heaters. Figure 5.9 shows the control logic to determine whether it
is currently heating or cooling season. During periods where the energy for heating
is higher than the energy for cooling, Cooling is set to 0 and for the reverse case,
Cooling is set to 1. For periods with no energy use for room conditioning, Cooling
is set to 2.
Discharging of the PCM ceiling should only take place when the occupants are
not present, since it may involve low temperatures of the ventilation air. Figure 5.10
shows the control logic that determines if occupants are currently present, in which
case Work is set to 1. The inputs Work_start and Work_end are set to 08:00 and
16:00, respectively. Only Monday through Friday are considered work days.
Figure 5.11 shows the control logic to determine whether the PCM ceiling is
currently discharged. As long as the average temperature through the thickness of
the PCM ceiling is above the lower freezing temperature, T_FE, the control system

Figure 5.12 shows the control logic that determines if there is free cooling po-
tential (FCP) of the PCM ceiling. First the FCP logic checks if the heat exchanger
can deliver a temperature, T_HX_max, that is higher than the room dew point,
in order to avoid condensation in the room. If this is not possible, then FCP is
set to -2, indicating too high humidity in the room. Next the control logic checks
if the ambient temperature is suﬃciently cold to discharge the PCM ceiling. If the
ambient air is not at least 2◦C (T_db) colder than the PCM, the FCP parameter
is set to -1, indicating too hot ambient conditions. If neither moisture nor ambient
air temperature is a problem then FCP is set to 1, indicating that free cooling is
possible.

supply temperature set point is at the upper limit of the comfort range in order

temperature is reached. If this cannot be accomplished by the heat exchanger alone,
then the heating coil is activated.

cooling (FCP = 1), the ﬂow rate is increased to 60 l/s to take advantage of the cold

other cases.

The ventilation system has a heat exchanger, where the fraction of return ﬂow,
Q_HX, from the room can be adjusted, and the rest is exhausted directly to the
outside. Figure 5.17 shows the control logic that regulates the ratio of indoor air
through the heat exchanger, when the operative temperature is controlled.
The
idea is to have maximum heat recovery when it beneﬁts the indoor climate, e.g.
comfortable temperature inside and low temperature outside, and completely bypass
if the cold air of the outside can cool the room. When bypassing, a check is made,
if the temperature is above the dew point of the most humid room. If not, then the
heat exchanger heats the supply air.

rather simple control system with night ventilation was implemented in the reference

that determines when night ventilation (NV ) is available. When a room is in cooling
season and no room is in heating season, NV is set to 1.
The air ﬂow rate to each room is determined as shown in Figure 5.19. The air
ﬂow rate is increased in cooling season during night time, if the room temperature
is higher than the lower comfort limit and higher than the ambient air temperature.
During oﬀ-hours when in cooling season (Cooling = 1) the night ventilation is turned
on, if the room temperature is above the lower comfort limit. The night ventilation
ﬂow rate is set to 60 l/s.
Figure 5.20 shows the control logic that chooses the supply temperature set point

in the ventilation control system. Figure 5.21 shows how the common supply tem-
perature is decided. If night ventilation is allowed (NV = 1), then the minimum set
point of the two rooms is used. Otherwise, an average of the two setpoints is used.

5.3
Set up of model in TRNSYS
The model is set up in TRNSYS in almost the same way as the simulation of the
climate chamber, see Figure 5.22 for the components and connections of the overall
system and Figure 5.23 for the control system. Type56 is used to model the building
thermal zones. Each oﬃce consists of two thermal zones; the oﬃce space and the
ventilation plenum.
Ventilation air is supplied to the ventilation plenum, and a
coupling air ﬂow delivers it to the oﬃce space. The hallway is a separate thermal
zone.
There are only two external walls in the model, one facing south and one facing
north, each having a full-width window. East and west-oriented walls, as well as
the ﬂoor and ceiling in the hallway, have adiabatic boundaries. The ﬂoor slab in
the oﬃce is adjacent to the ventilation plenum on the ﬂoor below, to simulate a
==PAGE:74==
62

Figure 5.22: Simulation Studio with components and connections used for the para-

multi-storey building.
The convective heat transfer coeﬃcient is 17.8 W/m2K on the two external
surfaces, 3.06 W/m2K on all internal non-PCM surfaces and 5.0 W/m2K on the
PCM surfaces.
==PAGE:75==
5.3. SET UP OF MODEL IN TRNSYS
63
The spectral data for the two window sizes are generated by using a piece of
software called Window v. 7.4 (Mitchell et al., 2016), developed by Lawrence Berke-
ley National Laboratory (LBL). The Pilkington 6C(66)-18Ar-4-18Ar-S(3) glazing is
modelled as close as possible using the available options in the glazing database of
the LBL software. The spectral and thermal data are inserted into the windows data-
base of TRNSYS. The spectral data can be found in Appendix M. The calculated
center U-value is 0.53 W/m2K and the center g-value is 0.283. The frame-to-window
ratio is 0.17 for the 5.25 m2 window and 0.13 for the 7.88 m2 window.
During the set up of the model, it was discovered that only one instance of
Type399 can exist in the same simulation project.
If two or more instances are
added, the simulation will run without warnings, but the instances share internal
variables and will give the same results for temperature, heat ﬂux etc. Two instances
are required, one for each PCM ceiling. This was accomplished by using Type313
in one oﬃce and Type399 in the other1.
Changing a set point based on e.g. a direct temperature comparison can make
the system unstable and prevent the model to converge in some time steps.
As
an example can be mentioned the way in which the discharge state of the PCM is
determined and how it aﬀects the ventilation ﬂow rate. When the PCM temperature
drops below the freezing temperature, the ventilation rate decreases from 60 l/s to
14.7 l/s, which in turn may increase the PCM temperature, since the PCM is cooled
less. This mechanism can make the PCM switch to and from the discharged state
very fast, resulting in equally fast changes in the ventilation ﬂow rate. In order to
prevent these kinds of instabilities, several Type973 comparators with a 1◦C dead
band have been used in the control system. Also to promote robustness, Type939
has been used to calculate the seven day moving average of the room heating and
cooling energy, which is used to determine whether an oﬃce is in heating or cooling
season.
As described in section 5.2.6 (Control systems), the ventilation supply temperat-
ure is either controlled directly or indirectly by monitoring the operative temperat-
ure in the oﬃces. The iterative feedback controller, Type22, takes care of the direct
control of the supply temperature, whereas a control logic is implemented in the
equation environment "Control common" to regulate the supply temperature based
on the operative temperatures.
Type752f and Type754f are used to model the cooling and heating coil in the
ventilation system. In the ﬁnal model, the cooling coil is permanently turned oﬀ. Its
function was originally to cool the ventilation air if the ambient air could not be used
directly to discharge the PCM ceiling, but this was not an energy eﬃcient solution.
Type91 is used to model the heat exchanger with a temperature eﬀectiveness of .90.
Type33d is used to calculate the moisture content of the supply air after it is heated
or cooled in the heat exchanger and to calculate the density of the supply air, which
is needed since only a mass ﬂow rate can be speciﬁed to the building. Type33d is
also used to calculate the dew point temperature in each oﬃce, which is used in the
control system to prevent condensation.
1Another solution would be to re-compile the FORTRAN source code into a new .dll ﬁle with
a diﬀerent Type number.
==PAGE:76==
64
CHAPTER 5. PARAMETRIC STUDY
5.3.1
Running the parameter variations
In this section is presented how to do a parameter variation in TRNSYS. In Simula-
tion Studio, all the parameters to be varied can conveniently be kept in one equation
environment, called "Parameter variations" in Figure 5.22. The parameters work like
variables in a programming language and can be used as inputs and parameters in
other Types.
The weather processor, multi-zone building, and the two PCM components all
rely on external data ﬁles that are varied between runs. An external ﬁle is referenced
by its unique handle, called a logical unit, or LU for short. A TRNSYS input ﬁle
(.dck-ﬁle) is generated and opened in TRNEdit. In the .dck-ﬁle the parameters to be
varied are grouped under "EQUATIONS n" where n is the number of parameters.
"EQUATIONS" is changed to "CONSTANTS" and then the parameter table can
be set up by choosing which parameters to vary and how many runs to calculate,
here 2240 runs.
Instead of manually putting in the data for all runs, a Matlab
script was created to generate an Excel ﬁle containing the parametric table, see
Appendix N for the script.
The table in Excel is simply copied into TRNEdit,
then a .dck ﬁle is automatically created for each run which are then supposed to
be automatically calculated in sequence, generating a separate output ﬁle for each
run. However, the authors discovered that when TRNEdit creates those 2240 .dck-
ﬁles, the number format of the parameters is changed from decimal to scientiﬁc - a
format that TRNSYS itself cannot read correctly, rendering the automatic sequential
calculation of the runs, useless. A Matlab script was created to convert the number
format back to decimal point in all the ﬁles, see Appendix O for the script. The
.dck ﬁles can be calculated sequentially from command line in Windows Command
Prompt by using a for-loop inside a .bat ﬁle. However, when running the ﬁles like
this, two warning dialogues pop up every time a new .dck-ﬁle is loaded, which have
to be clicked manually to start the calculation. Since this is not feasible to do 2240
times, a Microsoft Visual Basic script (VBS script) was created to wrap around the
process, enabling automatic closing of the dialogues using the Microsoft Windows
Script Host (WSH) methods, see Appendix P for the script. Each simulation took
about four minutes to calculate, amounting to more than six days of calculation
time.
==PAGE:77==
5.4. RESULTS
65
5.4
Results
Many of the simulation models using the Singapore climate ﬁle had problems with
convergence, and were often terminated before the whole simulation was completed.
The models were not re-calculated, so the results section only concern the other ﬁve
climates. 2240 simulations with PCM and 20 reference cases without PCM were
simulated. The output ﬁles have a total size of 141 GB, and can be found enclosed
on the USB ﬂash drive.
In the following section is presented a detailed analysis, which describes the best
design cases in each climate and describes in detail what is going on, when PCM is
incorporated in buildings. After that, a statistical analysis of the energy savings is
carried out on the whole data set.
5.4.1
Detailed analysis
The analysis was broken into a study of annual summaries and a graphical repres-
entation for the impact when varying diﬀerent parameters. A Matlab script was
developed to calculate the annual summaries of energy use, average latent heat
storage and average PCM utilisation, Appendix Q. Another Matlab script was de-
veloped to create plots of diﬀerent parameter variations, Appendix R. The Matlab
script automatically ﬁnds and plots results for user speciﬁed parameter levels, based
on 2240 results ﬁles.
Calculation of energy
Five outputs were logged for power related to heating and cooling: Pheat,S and
Pcool,S [W] are the power delivered by the room heater and cooler in Oﬃce South,
Pheat,N and Pcool,N [W] are the equivalents for Oﬃce North and PHC [W] is the
power delivered by the central heating coil in the ventilation system. The air ﬂow
rates to each oﬃce were also logged, Qair,S and Qair,N [m3/s] for Oﬃce South and
North, respectively
A speciﬁc fan power (SFP) was required, in order to translate air ﬂow into
electrical power. Exhausto’s online Quick Select tool (Exhausto, n.d.) was used to
estimate a SFP. All the simulations had an average air ﬂow rate of 95.5 m3/h. The
SFP was calculated for an air handling unit servicing 20 identical oﬃces with a total
air ﬂow rate of 1830 m3/h, with a pressure loss of 250 Pa. Exhausto’s calculation tool
estimated a SFP of 1456 J/m3, which was rounded to 1500 J/m3in this analysis. The
total energy use was calculated with equation 5.5, using a coeﬃcient of performance
(COP) of 1 on heating and cooling energy use, treating energy use for heating and
cooling equally with energy use for ventilation.
ETotal = ∆t
1000 ·

(ΣQair,S + ΣQair,N) · SFP+
(ΣPheat,S + ΣPcool,S + ΣPheat,N + ΣPcool,N + ΣPHC) · COP

(5.5)
Where ETotal [kWh] is the annual energy use for heating, cooling and ventilation,
∆t [h] is the data logging interval of 0.1 hour and division by 1000 converts into kW.
Each power is summed up over a whole year.
==PAGE:78==
66
CHAPTER 5. PARAMETRIC STUDY
The energy savings (ENS) were calculated as the relative diﬀerence in energy
use between the PCM and the corresponding reference case, equation 5.6.
ENS = ETotal,ref −ETotal,PCM
ETotal,ref
· 100%
(5.6)
Where ETotal,ref and ETotal,PCM [kWh] are the annual energy use for reference and
PCM case, respectively.
Calculation of energy storage and latent energy utilisation
The stored energy, Estored [Wh] in the PCM ceiling was estimated as the accumula-
tion of energy during the working hours (8:00-16:00), equation 5.7.
Estored = ∆t ·
 16
X
h=8
−QPCM,up −QPCM,down
!
(5.7)
Where ∆t [h] is the data logging interval of 0.1 hour, QPCM,up [W] is the thermal
power exiting the top surface and QPCM,down [w] is the thermal power exiting the
bottom surface, towards the oﬃce.
To estimate to which degree the PCM was used, the ratio of latent energy util-
isation (UR) was calculated as the ratio between the stored latent energy during a
day and the total possible latent energy storage, equation 5.8.
UR = Estored −A · d · ρ · ∆T · cp · 1/3600
A · d · ρ · LH · 1/3600
(5.8)
Where Estored [Wh] is the daily stored energy, A [m2] is the area of the PCM ceiling,
d [m] is thickness of the PCM boards, ρ [kg/m3] is the density of the PCM board
(1000 kg/m3), cp [J/kgK] is sensible speciﬁc heat capacity (1800 J/kgK) and LH
[J/kg] is the latent heat of the PCM. Division by 3600 converts from J to Wh.
The daily stored energy and utilisation ratio were calculated for each workday
and presented as an annual average.
Results on highest energy savings
In Table 5.5 is presented the total energy use, energy savings and the utilisation ratio
for the cases with highest energy savings for each climate, window size and internal
heat gain. The highest energy savings were found for full PCM ceiling, but the
results for half PCM ceiling are presented as well, using the same PCM properties.
The simulation showed a large potential for energy savings for all climates, in
the range 14.5-25.7% for full ceiling and 6.8-16.5% for half ceiling. On average, the
largest potential for energy savings were found for cases with large heat loads. The
most prevalent PCM types for high energy savings were PCM_012 (Copenhagen,
Madrid, Santa Maria, most cases in Buenos Aires) and PCM0_013 (most cases in
Dori and one case for Buenos Aires). Both PCM types had a large latent heat of
80 kJ/kg, as expected. The PCM with the highest melting temperature was the
best choice in Dori and Buenos Aires, which correlates with the high mean outdoor
temperature of these climates.

In all load cases, a PCM thickness of 0.02 m gave the highest energy savings, due
to increased latent energy compared to a thickness of 0.01 m. All cases had a low UR,
with a maximum of 29%. In an attempt to increase the UR, the eﬀect of reducing
the thickness was investigated. By reducing the thickness to 0.01 m for Copenhagen
with EGA=0.059, HG=L and a full PCM ceiling, the ENS decreased by 4%-points,
but UR S increased only by 5%-points. Decreasing the thickness reduced the ENS,
instead of realising more of the unused latent energy potential. The same tendency
was observed for the remaining cases. Therefore, the cause for the low UR might
be explained by the indoor temperature not ﬂuctuating suﬃciently to activate the
latent energy in the whole phase change range.
There was no clear pattern concerning thermal conductivity, except that both
Dori and Copenhagen had highest ENS for a thermal conductivity of 0.4 W/mK
and the remaining climates changed between 0.2 and 0.4 W/mK. This is in line with
==PAGE:80==
68
CHAPTER 5. PARAMETRIC STUDY
the statistical analysis, which found the thermal conductivity to be insigniﬁcant.
In Figure 5.24, the energy uses for Copenhagen and Dori are shown, separated
into room heaters and coolers, the heating coil and fan electricity for the case with
HG=H and EGA=0.092.

Figure 5.24: Distribution of energy use for HG=H and EGA=0.092 for Copenhagen
and Dori.
For Copenhagen, Figure 5.24a, most of the energy was used by the ventilation
system. The PCM case required a lower amount of air to provide suﬃcient cooling
during the day, compared to the reference case. The room heater was the second
largest energy using component, and the addition of PCM reduced the heating
energy use by 2.4 kWh/m2 during the winter. Almost no energy was needed for
cooling in the reference case (0.7 kWh/m2), and this was reduced to almost zero
with addition of PCM.
For Dori, Figure 5.24b, the cooling energy use was much higher and the PCM
provided larger reductions. The cooling energy use was reduced from 56.1 kWh/m2
to 32.9 kWh/m2 for the south oﬃce and from 40.0 kWh/m2 to 36.6 kWh/m2 for the
north oﬃce.
The heating coil was not activated in Dori nor in Copenhagen. The heating coil
only activates if the heat exchanger cannot heat the outside air above the dew point
temperature of the oﬃce. The occasions where this could happen are rare, since
the moisture content in the ambient air, and thus in the indoor air is low during
the winter in Denmark. Likewise, the climate of Dori is very arid, with all-year low
moisture content in the ambient air.

the climate or internal heat gains.

formed the reference cases. Copenhagen, Madrid and Santa Maria had higher energy

The case with the highest utilisation ratio is presented in Figure 5.26. The util-
isation ﬂuctuates during the year. The highest utilisation is found during winter,
with values of 60-80%. URs of 20-50% are found during summer. The utilisation
is low during summer because the discharging is not suﬃcient during night time,
due to high ambient temperatures. During winter with low ambient nocturnal tem-
peratures, the PCM can be cooled to lower temperatures, making it possible to use
more of the latent enthalpy.

Figure 5.27: Cumulated energy use for diﬀerent values of PCM area and thickness

Changing from no PCM to half to full PCM ceiling gave a reduction in energy
use of approximately 4.5 kWh/m2 for each change, Figure 5.27a. The cumulation
==PAGE:84==
72
CHAPTER 5. PARAMETRIC STUDY
curves in the PCM cases diverge from the reference case from the beginning, because
the PCM cases have a lower heating energy use. From July, the case with half PCM
ceiling starts to use energy at a higher rate, due to a higher ventilation rate. From
October, the half PCM ceiling case uses even more energy because of extra heating
energy use.
Changing the board thickness has a similar tendency, Figure 5.27b. The gap
between the two boards is more narrow during the spring and summer. The energy
use is reduced by 6.2 kWh/m2 for a thickness of 0.01 m and 9.3 kWh/m2 for a
thickness of 0.02 m. The reduction in energy use diminishes when the thickness is
increased, maybe due to a lower penetration depth and lower utilisation.

Figure 5.28: Air temperature in Oﬃce S in a period where cooling is activated

Cooling is activated on the 35th day in response to the air temperature reaching
27◦C, which is the upper limit of the thermal comfort range for Santa Maria. The
reference case has larger ﬂuctuations than the PCM case, see e.g. day 38-40. This
illustrates how the PCM ceiling stabilises the air temperature with the peak-shaving
eﬀect. At day 45, the air temperature reached 27◦C in the reference case; one day
earlier than the PCM case.

Figure 5.29a shows the reference case being cooled to about 20◦C whereas the
PCM cases are cooled further due to the diﬀerence in control. The reference case
prioritises cooling until the operative temperature is 1◦C higher than the lower
thermal comfort limit. The PCM cases prioritise cooling based on the discharge
state of the PCM.
On the 266th day, the eﬀect of PCM is visible.
The reference case and full
PCM ceiling case both have a room temperature of 20◦C around 08:00. During
the day, the operative temperature increases by 5◦C for the reference and 4.1◦C for
the full ceiling case. The half ceiling has the highest room temperature increase of
5.1◦C, but this case also starts out at a lower temperature. The ventilation rate
diﬀers between cases, due to the diﬀerent demands of the ceiling, Figure 5.29b. The
diﬀerence in ventilation rate explains why the temperature decreases more for the
full PCM ceiling case during the night between day 266 and 267.
Economy
In this section, the economy in incorporating PCM enhanced ceiling is analysed.
Table 5.30 details the energy savings, cost of saved energy (calculated based on the
Danish energy price of 0.30 e/kWh (Dong energy, 2016)) and the payback time
based on the cheapest of the products (70 e/m2), presented in section 2.2.3. The
results are presented with a coeﬃcient of performance (COP) of 1 (corresponding
to heating and cooling delivered by electricity) and 5 (corresponding to heating and
cooling delivered by a modern heat pump).
An increase of the COP factor from 1 to 5 reduces the amount of energy saved on
heating and cooling by 2-75% depending on climate and distribution of energy use.
Even though it is possible to have high energy savings with a PCM ceiling, the initial
cost of the PCM is too high in most cases for PCM to be a feasible investment. The

payback times for Copenhagen and Santa Maria were very long. The best cases with
a COP of 1, had a payback time between 19 and 23 years. A COP of 5 increased
the payback time to at least 48 years. An investment with this time frame is not
attractive, especially when also considering unknown long term deterioration and
costs associated with maintenance. PCM ceilings could be used in Buenos Aires,
Dori and Madrid, where the payback time were as low as 20, 14 and 32 years for
COP 5.
Retroﬁtting of PCM boards in existing buildings is another possible use of PCM.
One example is when extra insulation is retroﬁtted in old, historical buildings. This
reduces the thermal mass drastically, and PCM might provide a way to stabilize the
indoor climate, and possibly save energy. The economy in cases like this might be
better and would need a separate analysis.

5.4. RESULTS

5.4.2
Statistical analysis
A statistical analysis is carried out on the complete dataset from the parametric
study. The goal is to ﬁnd the parameters’ inﬂuence on the energy savings (ENS)
and to make a statistical model that can be used in design. The statistical analysis
is for ENS-values calculated with a COP on heating and cooling of 1.
As was
mentioned in section 5.4.1, the COP-value has an impact on how much energy is
saved. The statistical analysis and ﬁgures are created using the free open-source
statistical software called R (R Core Team, 2016).
The percentage energy savings associated with each run is presented are Figure
5.31 as a density histogram. The data follow approximately a normal distribution
with µ = 4.11 and σ = 6.50, which is also plotted for reference. It is evident that
the energy use is almost as likely to increase (negative energy saving) as it is to
decrease, if the PCM ceiling is not designed carefully. However, a good design can
lead to considerable energy savings.
−20
−10
0
10
20
30
0.00
0.04
0.08
0.12
Mean = 4.11, std = 6.50
Energy savings [%]
Density [−]
Figure 5.31: Histogram of the energy savings, which approximately follows a normal
distribution.
A boxplot for the energy savings in each climate is presented in Figure 5.32. The
boxplot shows how the ﬁve climates diﬀer in the energy savings potential. According
to this parametric study, no combination of parameters yielded an increase in energy
use in Buenos Aires (AR) nor Dori (BF). The results for Madrid (ES) shows a
minimum energy saving of -4.2%, meaning a slight increase in energy use for some
parameter combinations. The climates of Copenhagen (DK) and Santa Maria (US)
can have either a large increase or decrease in energy use, depending on the design.
All climates can potentially have energy savings of up to 20-25%. It is also noted
that the distribution is diﬀerent between the climates. Only the results for US are
approximately normally distributed, while the other climates are skewed right. The
results for Dori have a very strong skewness with many outliers, meaning that most
runs yielded only a small energy saving.
A linear model for each climate has been ﬁtted to describe energy savings (ENS).
In some of the models, the dependent variable, ENS, has been transformed, due the
the diﬀerence in distribution. Transforming the data brings the distribution closer
==PAGE:88==
76
CHAPTER 5. PARAMETRIC STUDY
AR
BF
DK
ES
US
−20
−10
0
10
20
30
Energy savings [%]
Figure 5.32: Boxplot of the energy savings, grouped by climate.
to a normal distribution, it improves homoscedasticity2 and it improves the linear
relationships assumed in the linear models. The data for BF were transformed by
taking the square root to ENS and then the reciprocal value. This is a very strong
power-transformation that brings the large outliers in to form a more symmetrical
distribution.
The data for AR were transformed by taking log10 to ENS. This
transformation also brings in outliers and creates a more symmetrical distribution,
but the eﬀect is less pronounced. The data for ES and DK could have beneﬁted
from a transformation as well, but this was not done, since negative values occur in
the datasets, which complicates a transformation when data are also skewed right.
The linear models are presented in equation 5.9-5.11, where Heat_Gain [W/m2]
is the total internal heat gains, EGA [-] is the equivalent glazing area, equation 5.3,
PCM_Area [-] is the fraction of the ceiling area covered with PCM boards, PCM_d
[m] is the thickness of the PCM boards and PCM refers to one of the fourteen
simulated cp-curves, see Appendix I. The thermal conductivity was not included
in the models, because the eﬀect was found insigniﬁcant.
No interaction terms
were signiﬁcant either, except climate. The linear regression estimates the eﬀect
of each parameter grouped by climate. The grouping makes sure that interaction
between the climate and all other parameters are taken into account by the model.
The estimates of the regression coeﬃcients are shown in Table 5.8 along with the
signiﬁcance level for each parameter.
ENS ∼Heat_Gain + EGA + PCM_Area + PCM_d + PCM
(5.9)
log10 (ENS) ∼Heat_Gain + EGA + PCM_Area + PCM_d + PCM
(5.10)
1/ENS0.5 ∼Heat_Gain + EGA + PCM_Area + PCM_d + PCM
(5.11)
2This word means even variance or dispersion. This is assessed in residual plots, where the
residuals should appear random.

Table 5.8 shows that Heat_Gain for the climate of DK was not signiﬁcant, but
the parameter was included nevertheless. The regression coeﬃcients can be used
to predict energy savings in one of the parametric runs, or even in a design that
diﬀers slightly. An example is shown below for DK, with an internal heat load of
16.8 W/m2, PCM_002 boards with thickness of 20 mm covering the whole ceiling
and a EGA-value of 0.059. Using the statistical model results in a energy saving of
8.9%. The simulation model with the same parameters gave an energy savings of
7.6-8.1% depending on the thermal conductivity of the boards, so this is an example
of a good estimate.
ENS = −5.51 −0.0534 · 16.8 −79.4 · 0.059 + 3.47 · 1.0 + 111 · 0.02 + 14.3 = 8.9%
To visualize how well the estimates are for all the simulated runs, the ﬁtted ENS
is plotted against the simulated ENS for all ﬁve climates, Figure 5.33. The adjusted
R2-values are shown as a measure of how accurate the model predicts the simulated
values. The R2-values are in the range [0.78; 0.98] which indicates a quite good
model. The R2-value can only be trusted if the corresponding residual plot is free
from bias, Figure 5.34. The residuals should optimally appear random, which is the
case for AR, BF and US. The datasets of DK and ES, which could have beneﬁted
from a data transformation, show some bias in the residual plots.
In linear regression it is assumed that the residuals are normally distributed.
This assumption is checked with QQ-plots (Quantile-Quantile plots), Figure 5.35.
==PAGE:90==
78
CHAPTER 5. PARAMETRIC STUDY
The standardized residuals should follow the quantiles of the standard normal dis-
tribution to conclude normality of the residuals. There are not any big violations
of the normality assumption, but again it is seen that ES and especially DK could
have beneﬁted from a data transformation.
This statistical analysis gave a overview of the data and how it varies, depending
on each of the parameters from the parametric study. It was found that thermal
conductivity of the PCM board was not signiﬁcant. One model for each climate
was created, since there were interaction between the climate and most of the other
parameters. The linear models predict the energy savings potential with a COP
value of 1 on the heating and cooling energy use. Depending on the actual COP-
value, the energy savings might be less. This is discussed more in the next section.

==PAGE:95==
Chapter 6
Conclusion
The parametric study determined six climates to investigate for application of PCM;
Buenos Aires, Dori, Copenhagen, Madrid, Singapore and Santa Maria. The climates
have diﬀerent diurnal temperature ranges and average annual temperature, ensuring
a wide variation in climatic conditions. 14 distinct PCM types were created with
phase change temperatures spanning 15-32◦C and latent enthalpy of 40 and 80
kJ/kg. The load case was also varied by changing the window size and the internal
heat gains.
Validations of the TRNSYS simulation models were performed against the Mi-
crobat benchmark test and experimental results from a climate chamber. The ex-
periments were conducted for diﬀerent settings of ventilation rate and occupancy.
Both validation models accurately predicted the PCM and air temperatures during
increasing temperatures. Higher discrepancies were found during cooling. In the
climate chamber validation model, the discrepancy could be caused by hysteresis in
the eﬀective heat capacity, not being taken into account.
The experiments from the climate chamber showed that a comfortable thermal
environment can be achieved for at least two occupants. The utilisation ratio of
the latent enthalpy depended strongly on the load case and discharge strategy. To
have a high utilisation, the discharging strategy should be tailored to the level of
heat gains in the room. A utilisation ratio of 97% was achieved for a two-occupant
scenario with embedded pipes for discharging. The maximum stored latent heat was
23.6 Wh/m2.
The parametric study showed that incorporation of PCM into an oﬃce environ-
ment provides a potential for energy savings. Even for the well-insulated oﬃce, the
energy use could be reduced by 14.5-25.6% for a full PCM ceiling, depending on the
climate and load case. The energy saving potentials in Buenos Aires, Copenhagen,
Madrid and Santa Maria are comparable and have an average reduction in annual
energy use of 7.9 kWh/m2 for a full PCM ceiling. Dori has a much higher potential
for energy savings of 29 kWh/m2. For the climates of Copenhagen and Santa Maria,
the potential for energy savings was accompanied by an equal risk of energy increase,
if the chosen PCM does not ﬁt the climate or load case.
When heating and cooling are delivered with a COP of 5, the payback time for
the PCM ceiling is over 48 years for Copenhagen and Santa Maria. The payback
time is less than 14 years in the climate of Dori, 30 years for Buenos Aires and 39
years for Madrid, making it a possible choice for a long term investment. Covering
83
==PAGE:96==
84
CHAPTER 6. CONCLUSION
only half of the ceiling with PCM was more proﬁtable than having a full ceiling, due
to a diminishing increase in energy savings by a larger ceiling. If the thermal energy
is delivered with a COP-value less than 5, the energy savings increase. With a COP
of 1, the payback time is reduced to 6 years for Dori and 17-23 years other climates.
Climates like Dori seem to really beneﬁt from PCM.
The simulation showed that the utilisation ratio was strongly dependent on the
seasons. In the case with highest utilisation, the PCM ceiling used 60-80% of the
available latent heat during winter and 20-50% during summer, resulting in a large
unused latent energy for most of the year.
The reason was that the PCM was
not fully discharged during summer. Embedded pipes might be more eﬀective at
discharging, and it would be less dependent on climate and seasonality.
The choice of simulating many diﬀerent parameters resulted in results with a low
resolution. This is good for rough assessments of the applicability of PCM, but the
results have too few levels for predicting the optimal PCM ceiling or predicting the
energy savings with good accuracy. The statistical models, made on the basis of the
parametric study, can be used give a rough estimate on energy savings in diﬀerent
climates and load cases. A few general guidelines on PCM are presented below.
6.1
Guidelines
• The PCM types that provided the highest energy savings, had the peak of the
eﬀective heat capacity curve shifted towards the upper comfort range limit.
• For climates with heating energy use, having the peak of the eﬀective heat
capacity curve shifted towards the lower comfort range limit, increased energy
use for heating resulting in negative energy savings.
• A small amount of PCM is more proﬁtable, because of diminishing energy
savings when the PCM amount increases.
• A high latent heat capacity increases the eﬀect of the PCM system, i.e. both
positive and negative values of ENS (energy savings) are augmented. That is,
in a poor design, a higher latent heat will also increase energy use.
6.2
Suggestions for future work
It would be very interesting to look into the energy savings and economy associated
with retroﬁtting PCM boards into existing building. Especially in conjunction with
energy refurbishments in heritage buildings or other buildings where insulation is
added to the internal surface of the outer walls.
Another suggestion is to make a similar study as presented in this thesis, but with
fewer parameter to vary. This would make it possible to have a higher resolution on
the parameters, possibly leading to a better insight into how the parameters aﬀect
the energy use and thermal environment.
It could be interesting to make a similar study, but with discharging by embedded
pipes in the PCM boards.
This could make the solution less dependent on the
climate, making PCM systems proﬁtable to a wider range of climates.

Appendix C
Guide on how to set up time
schedules in the climate
chamber 6 control system
In order to help other operators of chamber 6 save time, a guide for calculating
schedule time points is attached here.
There are two steps:
• Each number from the time format "hh:mm:ss:ms" is converted into a 4-digit
binary-coded decimal (BCD).
• All binary-coded decimals are joined into one binary number and converted
from binary back into a decimal number.
In Figure C.1 is shown an example where 09:30:00:00 is converted into a joined
binary-coded decimal.
00001001 0011 0000 0000 00000000 0000
Figure C.1: Example of the conversion of time into BCD.
The BCD 00001001001100000000000000000000 is then converted into the decimal
number 154140672, and this is the schedule that have to be applied in the controls
for chamber 6.

Appendix G
Convergence for climate
chamber experiments, scenario
2-5
The convergence for the remaining four scenarios is analysed on the basis of air
temperature and average temperature of the PCM boards.
G.1
Scenario 2
Based on the results in Figure G.1, there is a slight increase in temperatures from
initiation to the following days. The 2nd and 4th of April are slightly warmer than
the 3rd of April, but all days are considered converged and are valid representations
for the scenario. 3rd of April is picked as the representative day for scenario 2, due
to a bump in the air temperature curve during the 4th of April.

G.2
Scenario 3
Based on the results in Figure G.2, there is an increase in temperatures from the
two ﬁrst days to the rest. From the 21st of April, the temperatures appear to have
converged. The 23rd of April is picked as the representative day for scenario 3.

G.3
Scenario 4
Based on the results in Figure G.3, decreasing peak temperatures are observed for
the 15th and 16th of April. From the 17th of April, the temperatures have converged.
The 18th of April is selected as the representative day for scenario 4.
G.4
Scenario 5
Based on the results in Figure G.4, increasing peak temperatures are observed from
the 8th of April to the following day. The remaining two days are considered con-
verged. The 10th of April is picked as the representative day for scenario 4.

Appendix H
Matlab script: Eﬀective heat
capacity curves
117
==PAGE:130==
E:\Scripts\Cp curve calculator\Cp_curve_calc.m
1 of 4
    clc
    clear
    %Delete existing files in the output folders
    delete('CpFreezingDetailed/*.txt')
    delete('CpMeltingDetailed/*.txt')
    delete('CpFreezing16/*.txt')
    delete('CpMelting16/*.txt')
    delete('figures/*.png')
 
    %Set up material parameters for which to make combinations
    dT=2; %[K] Temperature range for the linear part of Cp curve
    cp=1.8; %Specific heat capacity
    Ms=[16,20,24]; %Melting start
    Fs=[19,23,27]; %Freezing start
    PCTR=[6,8,10]; %Phase change temperature range
    LH=[40,80]; %Latent heat
    T_step=0.1; %Temperature step
    maxHysteresis=5;
    minHysteresis=1;
 
    %Relative vertical distance between Cp specific and Cp peak for calculating Ms and 
Fe
    S=0.5;
 
    %Make a matrix with all combinations
    [A,B,C,D] = ndgrid(Ms,Fs,PCTR,LH);
    Products = [A(:),B(:),C(:),D(:)];
 
    MeltingPeak=Products(:,1)+Products(:,3)-dT;
    FreezingPeak=Products(:,2)-dT;
    hysteresis=MeltingPeak-FreezingPeak;
 
    %Delete combinations where hysteresis is outside allowed range
    Products(:,5)=hysteresis;
    B=(Products(:,5)<=maxHysteresis & Products(:,5)>=minHysteresis);
    Products(B==0,:)=[];
 
    %Calculate all parameters Ms,Me,Mp,Fs,Fe,Fp,PCTR,LH
    MeltingStart=Products(:,1);
    MeltingEnd=Products(:,1)+Products(:,3);
    MeltingPeak=Products(:,1)+Products(:,3)-dT;
    FreezingStart=Products(:,2);
    FreezingEnd=Products(:,2)-Products(:,3);
    FreezingPeak=Products(:,2)-dT;
    productPCTR=Products(:,3);
    productLH=Products(:,4);
    hysteresis=MeltingPeak-FreezingPeak;
 
    %Number of combinations
    nProducts=size(Products,1);
 
    TPCM=(min(FreezingEnd)-10:T_step:max(MeltingEnd))';
    summary=zeros(nProducts,3);
 
    %Set error tolerance and max. iterations when finding curve constants
==PAGE:131==
E:\Scripts\Cp curve calculator\Cp_curve_calc.m
2 of 4
    toleranceLH=0.1;
    toleranceMs=0.1;
    maxIterations=10000;
 
    %Find curve constants for each combination
    for k=1:nProducts
        a=30;
        b=-0.02;
        g=-0.5;
        errorLH=100;
        errorMs=100;
        i=0;
        LHcalc=0;
        MsCalc=0;
        %Check if errors are small enough
        while (errorLH > toleranceLH || errorMs > toleranceMs) && i < maxIterations
            if LHcalc < (productLH(k)-toleranceLH)
                a=a+0.01;
            elseif LHcalc > (productLH(k)+toleranceLH)
                a=a-0.01;
            elseif MsCalc < (MeltingStart(k)-toleranceMs)
                b=b-0.01;
            elseif MsCalc > (MeltingStart(k)+toleranceMs)
                if b<-0.01
                    b=b+0.01;
                elseif g<-0.01
                    g=g+0.01;
                end
            end
 
            CpM=TPCM;
            Cppeak=0;
            Cppeakindex=0;
            %Fill the T,cp vector
            for n=1:size(CpM,1)
                if TPCM(n)<MeltingPeak(k)
                    CpM(n,2)=cp+(a)*(MeltingPeak(k)-TPCM(n))^(b)*exp(g*(MeltingPeak(k)
-TPCM(n)));
                elseif TPCM(n) == MeltingPeak(k)
                    CpM(n,2)=CpM(n-1,2);
                    Cppeak=CpM(n,2);
                    Cppeakindex=n;
                elseif TPCM(n) >= MeltingEnd(k)
                    CpM(n,2)=cp;
                else
                    CpM(n,2)=(cp-Cppeak)/dT*(TPCM(n)-MeltingPeak(k))+Cppeak;
                end
            end
            %Calculate the line defining Ms and Fe
            deltaCp=Cppeak-cp;
            CpMsFe=deltaCp*S+cp;
            [c, index] = min(abs(CpM(1:Cppeakindex,2)-CpMsFe));
            closestValues = CpM(index,2); % Finds first one only!
            dCpdT=-exp(g*(MeltingPeak(k)-TPCM(index)))*a*((MeltingPeak(k)-TPCM(index))
^(b-1)*b+(MeltingPeak(k)-TPCM(index))^b*g);
==PAGE:132==
E:\Scripts\Cp curve calculator\Cp_curve_calc.m
3 of 4
            lineMs=[min(CpM(:,1)),0;max(CpM(:,1)),0];
            lineMs(:,2)=lineMs(:,1)*dCpdT+CpM(index,2)-dCpdT*CpM(index,1);
 
            %Calculate error on LH and MS        
            LHcalc = trapz(CpM(:,1),CpM(:,2)-cp); %Numerical integration
            MsCalc=(cp-CpM(index,2)+dCpdT*CpM(index,1))/dCpdT;
            errorLH=abs(LHcalc-productLH(k));
            errorMs=abs(MsCalc-MeltingStart(k));
            i=i+1;
        end
 
        %Calculate corresponding freezing curve by horizontal displacement
        CpF=[CpM(:,1)-hysteresis(k),CpM(:,2)];
        CpF(1:hysteresis(k)/T_step,:)=[];
        q=zeros(hysteresis(k)/T_step,2);
        q(:,2)=cp;
        q(:,1)=max(CpF(:,1))+T_step:T_step:max(CpM(:,1));
        CpF(end+1:end+hysteresis(k)/T_step,:)=q;
 
        %Calculate simplified curve with just 16 points
        %Points are closer spaced near the peak
        CpM16=zeros(16,2);
        CpM16(1,:)=[CpM(1,1),CpM(1,2)];
        CpM16(15,:)=[MeltingPeak(k),Cppeak];
        x=(Cppeakindex/59.2);
        u=x;
        for m=1:13
            L=round(u);
            CpM16(15-m,:)=[CpM(Cppeakindex-L,1),CpM(Cppeakindex-L,2)];
            u=u+x*1.2^m;
        end
        %Corresponding simplified freezing curve
        CpM16(16,:)=[MeltingEnd(k),cp];
        CpF16=CpM16;
        CpF16(:,1)=CpF16(:,1)-hysteresis(k);
 
        %Print text files which are used as input in TRNSYS model
        %Melting curve, with a datapoint per T_step
        fileID = fopen( sprintf( 'CpMeltingDetailed/CpM_d%3.3d.txt',k),'w');
        fprintf(fileID,'%3.1f\t',CpM(:,1));
        fprintf(fileID,'\r\n');
        fprintf(fileID,'%5.0f\r\n',CpM(:,2)*1000);
        fclose(fileID);
        %Freezing curve, with a datapoint per T_step
        fileID = fopen( sprintf( 'CpFreezingDetailed/CpF_d%3.3d.txt',k),'w');
        fprintf(fileID,'%3.1f\t',CpF(:,1));
        fprintf(fileID,'\r\n');
        fprintf(fileID,'%5.0f\r\n',CpF(:,2)*1000);
        fclose(fileID);
        summary(k,1:3)=[errorMs,errorLH,i];
        %Melting curve, with 16 data points
        fileID = fopen( sprintf( 'CpMelting16/CpM_16%3.3d.txt',k),'w');
        fprintf(fileID,'%3.1f\t',CpM16(:,1));
        fprintf(fileID,'\r\n');
        fprintf(fileID,'%5.0f\r\n',CpM16(:,2)*1000);
==PAGE:133==
E:\Scripts\Cp curve calculator\Cp_curve_calc.m
4 of 4
        fclose(fileID);
        %Freezing curve, with 16 data points
        fileID = fopen( sprintf( 'CpFreezing16/CpF_16%3.3d.txt',k),'w');
        fprintf(fileID,'%3.1f\t',CpF16(:,1));
        fprintf(fileID,'\r\n');
        fprintf(fileID,'%5.0f\r\n',CpF16(:,2)*1000);
        fclose(fileID);
 
        %Set up figure and write to file
        set(gcf, 'units', 'centimeters', 'pos', [0 0 14 7])
        set(gcf,'PaperPositionMode','auto')
        plot(CpM(:,1),CpM(:,2),'r',CpF(:,1),CpF(:,2),'b')
        hTitle=title(sprintf('Tms=%2.0d, Tfs=%2.0d, PCTR=%2.0d, H=%2.0d, LH=%2.0d',
MeltingStart(k),FreezingStart(k),productPCTR(k),hysteresis(k),productLH(k)));
        set(hTitle,'FontSize',10,'fontweight','normal');
        xlabel('Temperature [C]','FontSize',10,'fontweight','normal') 
        ylabel('Effective heat capacity [J/g/K]','FontSize',10,'fontweight','normal')
        %legend('Melting','Freezing')
        xlim([0 35])
        ylim([0 26])
        print(sprintf( 'figures/Cp%3.3d',k),'-depsc','-r0')
 
        %Summary file and print current combination being processed
        summary(k,1:3)=[errorMs,errorLH,i];
        k
    end

Matlab script: Parameter table

    %Input_O_matic is a script to generate an excel table containing all combinations 
of the
    %different levels for the parameters described in the thesis, under the
    %section parametric study.
 
    clc
    clear
    %Input 
    start=201;      %First Logical Unit used to assign files
    NoClimat=6;     %Number of climate files
    NoPCM=14;       %Number of PCM files
    NoBuilding=4;
    Thickness=[0.01,0.02];
    HeatGains=[1,2];
    lambda=[0.2,0.4];
    %Assignment of logical units to climate files using variable "start"
    Climat=ones(NoClimat,1);
    for i=start:start+NoClimat-1
        Climat(i-start+1)=i;
    end
    %Assignment of logical units to PCM continuing from the end of the climate 
    %files
    PCM=ones(NoPCM,1);
    for j=i+1:i+NoPCM
        PCM(j-i)=j;
    end
    %Assignment of logical units to building continuing from the end of the 
    %PCM freezing files
    Building=ones(NoBuilding,1);
    for k=j+1:j+NoBuilding
        Building(k-j)=k;
    end
 
    %All possible combinations of the parameters are generated
    [A,B,C,D,E,F] = ndgrid(Climat,PCM,Building,Thickness,HeatGains,lambda);
    Products = [A(:),B(:),C(:),D(:),E(:),F(:)];
    Products=[Products(:,1:2),ones(2688,1),Products(:,3:6)];
    y=1;
    %Generation of logical unit numbers for the PCM melting curve
    for l=k+1:k+NoPCM
        MEPCM(y,1)=l;
        x=find(Products(:,2)==PCM(y,1));
        Products(x,3)=l;
        y=y+1;
    end
    %Creation of an item list for describing the climate files: names for the 
    %climate files, hemisphere orientation and thermal comfort range
    Climate_name={'Buenos Aires','Dori','Copenhagen','Madrid','Singapore','Santa 
Maria'};
    Climate_Hem=[-1,1,1,1,1,1];
    Climate_Comf=[20.4,28.4;22.9,29.9;19.3,26.1;20.1,27.4;23.5,31.5;19.3,27.0];
    for i=1:NoClimat
        Search=find([Products(:,1)]==Climat(i,1));
        Products(Search,8)=Climate_Hem(1,i);
        Products(Search,9)=Climate_Comf(i,1);
==PAGE:161==
E:\Scripts\Parametric analysis\Input_O_matic.m
2 of 3
        Products(Search,10)=Climate_Comf(i,2);
        Climate_print(Search,1)=Climate_name(1,i);
    end
    %Creation of an item list for describing the building files: PCM area 
    %and EGA
    Building_prop=[10.5,0.059;21,0.059;10.5,0.092;21,0.092];
    for i=1:NoBuilding
        Search=find([Products(:,4)]==Building(i,1));
        Products(Search,11)=Building_prop(i,1);
        Products(Search,12)=Building_prop(i,2);
    end
    %Creation of an item list for describing the PCM files: temperature of 
    %feezing and melting end and latent heat
    PCM_prop=[17 26 40;21 30 40;21 32 40;15 28 40;19 28 40;19 32 40;17 30 40;17 26 80;
21 30 80;21 32 80;15 28 80;19 28 80;19 32 80;17 30 80;0 100 0];
    PCM_prop(:,3)=PCM_prop(:,3)*1000;
    for i=1:NoPCM
        Search=find([Products(:,2)]==PCM(i,1));
        Products(Search,13)=PCM_prop(i,1);
        Products(Search,14)=PCM_prop(i,2);
        Products(Search,15)=PCM_prop(i,3);
    end
    %Writes header and all runs with description into an Excel file
    %called "ParameterInput.xlsx".
    textHeader = {'Climate LU', 'PCM LU FE','PCM LU ME', 'Building LU', 'PCM 
Thinkness', 'HeatGains', 'Heat Conductivity', 'Hemesphere', 'Lower comf range','Upper 
comf range','PCM Area','EGA','Freezing End','Melting End','Latent Heat','Climate 
Name'};
    xlswrite('ParameterInput.xlsx',Products,'Sheet1','A2')
    xlswrite('ParameterInput.xlsx',textHeader,'Sheet1','A1')
    xlswrite('ParameterInput.xlsx',Climate_print,'Sheet1','P2')
 
    %Generates a text file called "path_in.txt" with the path paired with 
    %logical unit numbers for associated files for the simulation: climate, 
    %building and PCM
    path(1,1)={sprintf( 'ASSIGN "Climate\\AR-Buenos-Aires-875850.tm2" 201\r\n')};
    path(2,1)={sprintf( 'ASSIGN "Climate\\BF-Dori-655010.tm2" 202\r\n')};
    path(3,1)={sprintf( 'ASSIGN "Climate\\DK-Kobenhavn-Taastrup-61800000.tm2" 
203\r\n')};
    path(4,1)={sprintf( 'ASSIGN "Climate\\ES-Madrid-Barajas-82210.tm2" 204\r\n')};
    path(5,1)={sprintf( 'ASSIGN "Climate\\SG-Singapore-Airp-486980000.tm2" 205\r\n')};
    path(6,1)={sprintf( 'ASSIGN "Climate\\US-CA-Santa-Maria-23273.tm2" 206\r\n')};
    for i=1:NoPCM
        x=PCM(i,1);

    %fix_format_O_matic is a script that finds all TRNSYS .dck files in the
    %current directory, opens each one, finds and changes the number format
    %in the varied parameters from scientific to decimal and saves the files
 
    clc
    clear
    %Number of files with .dck extension
    files = size(dir('**.DCK'),1);
    %Set line number where the first parameter is defined in the .dck files
    LineNoStart=42;
    LineNoEnd=LineNoStart+12; %13 parameters has their number format changed
 
    for k=1:files-1
        filenametxt=['OFFICE',num2str(k),'.txt'];
        filenamedck=['OFFICE',num2str(k),'.dck'];
        movefile(filenamedck,filenametxt);
        fid = fopen(filenametxt,'r');
        i = 1;
        tline = fgetl(fid);
        A{i} = tline;
        %Load the contents of the file into a matrix A
        while ischar(tline)
            i = i+1;
            tline = fgetl(fid);
            A{i} = tline;
        end
        fclose(fid);
        p=1;
        %Find the number in each parameter definition
        for i=LineNoStart:LineNoEnd
            get=cell2mat(A(i));
            l=size(get,2);
            numb=str2num(get(end-9:end));
            B(p,1)={strcat(get(1:end-10),num2str(numb))};
            p=p+1;
        end
        get=cell2mat(A(LineNoEnd+1));
        hem=get(1:15);
        %Separate treatment of the "hemisphere" parameter, since it has a
        %different format
        if get(16)=='-'
            B(14,1)={'PAR_HEMISPHERE=-1'};
        else
            B(14,1)={'PAR_HEMISPHERE=1'};
        end
        for i=LineNoStart:LineNoEnd+1
            A(i)=B(i-LineNoStart+1);
        end
        fid = fopen(filenametxt, 'w');
        for i = 1:numel(A)
            if A{i+1} == -1
                fprintf(fid,'%s', A{i});
                break
            else
                fprintf(fid,'%s\n', A{i});
==PAGE:165==
E:\Scripts\Parametric analysis\fix_format_O_matic.m
2 of 2
            end
        end
        fclose(fid);
        movefile(filenametxt,filenamedck);
        k
    end

Appendix P
Visual Basic script: Run .dck

E:\Scripts\Run_dck_files.vbs
18. juni 2016 13:48
'This Visual Basic script opens 2240 TRNSYS .dck files and calculates them
'in turn, while automatically dismissing two warning dialogues each time.
'This file is located on the USB drive E:\Scripts\Run_dck_files.vbs
'NOTE: To terminate the script before it finishes, open task manager and kill
'the process called "wscript.exe"
'create the Windows Scripting Host object
Set WshShell = WScript.CreateObject("WScript.Shell")
'Open Windows Command Prompt
WshShell.Run("C:\Windows\System32\cmd.exe")
WScript.Sleep 1000
'Each TRNSYS .dck file is opened and calculated in turn
For i = 1 to 2240
'Set focus to the Command Prompt window
WshShell.AppActivate "Administrator:"
'Use TRNExe.exe to open the next .dck file in a specified directory
WshShell.SendKeys "c:\Trnsys17\Exe\TRNexe.exe 
+2C:\Trnsys17\MyProjects\Office2\Office"&i&".dck+2 /n"
WshShell.SendKeys "%{Enter}"
WScript.Sleep 2000
'Set focus to the first warning dialogue and dismiss it
WshShell.AppActivate "TRNEXE: TRNExe.exe - System Error"
WScript.Sleep 100
WshShell.SendKeys "~"
WScript.Sleep 100
'Set focus to the second warning dialogue and dismiss it
WshShell.AppActivate "TRNEXE: TRNExe.exe - System Error"
WScript.Sleep 100
WshShell.SendKeys "~"
'Wait 4 minutes - the time it roughly takes to finish one simulation
WScript.Sleep 240000
Next
Wscript.Quit

        fid1=fopen(filename);
        B=textscan(fid1,'%f %f %f %f %f %f %f %f %f %f %f %f %f %f %f %f %f %f %f %f %
f %f %f %f %f', 'headerlines', 1); %Loads data to "A"
        B=cell2mat(B);
        B(:,26)=B(:,8)+B(:,9)+B(:,10)+B(:,11)+B(:,12)+B(:,13)+B(:,14)*SFP+B(:,15)*SFP;
        B(:,27)=cumsum(B(:,26))*0.1/1000;
        AccEPCM(1,15)=B(end,27);
        if What2plotX==1
            if AxisConstraintHigh-AxisConstraintLow <73
                X=(B(:,What2plotX));
            elseif AxisConstraintHigh-AxisConstraintLow <2191
                X=(B(:,What2plotX))/24;
                OverW=1;
            else
                X=(B(:,What2plotX))/730;
                OverW=2;
            end
            Y=(B(:,What2plotY));
        else
            X=(B(:,What2plotX));
            Y=(B(:,What2plotY));
        end
        Data2plotXR(:,i)=X(findS:findE);
        Data2plotYR(:,i)=Y(findS:findE);
        plot(Data2plotXR(:,i),Data2plotYR(:,i),'--','Color',Colors(i,:));
    end
    set(gca,'FontSize',FPsize)
    %Sets up legends and axis titles
    h_legend=legend(Leg2plot,'Location','northwest','Orientation','vertical');
    set(h_legend,'FontSize',FLsize);
    if OverW==0;
        xl=xlabel(textHeader(What2plotX))
    else
        HeaderTime={'Time [d]','Time [month]'};
        xl=xlabel(HeaderTime(OverW))
    end
    yl=ylabel(textHeader(What2plotY))
    set(xl,'FontSize',FPsize);
    set(yl,'FontSize',FPsize);
    %Limts the axis to span from the minium and maxmium X and Y value
    axis([min(min(min(Data2plotX),min(Data2plotXR))) max(max(max(Data2plotX),max
(Data2plotXR))) 18 max(max(max(Data2plotY),max(Data2plotYR)))])
    k = size(dir('figures/**.png'));
    k=k(1,1)+1;
    %Prints the figure to an .eps file
    print(FigureName,'-depsc','-r0')
    % Closes all opened .txt files
    fclose('all');
